CHELE
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
SMALL FARMER SERVICES PROJECT
I. LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:              Republic of Chile
Executina Agencies:   Ministry of Agriculture (MAG), Agricultural Development
Institute (INDAP) and Ministry of National Property (MBN)
Beneficiaries:         About 84,000 small farm families
Loan Amount:           US$95 million equivalent
Terms:                 Seventeen years, including five years of grace, with level
payments of principal, at the Bank's standard variable
interest rate.
Proiect Obiectives:   The project would: (a) overcome constraints to increased
small-farmer productivity and incomes and improved
well-being; (b) enhance the institutional capacity and
effectiveness of INDAP and MBN; and (c) foster small-farmer
access to commercial markets for their products and factors
of production, and to publicly-sponsored agricultural
development programs.
Project Description:  The project would include: (a) technology transfer, invclving
both farm-oriented extension and home-centered assistance
(family kitchen gardens, water treatment, improved diets and
cooking methods, awareness of education and health services);
(b) complementary credit for working capital (e.g., seed,
fertilizers) and investment (e.g., fencing, reproductive and
draft animals, orchard establishment, implements, on-farm
storage), consistent with technology transfer initiatives;
(c) land titling; (d) a rural communication system to improve
the dissemination of information related to small farmer
production and social services; and (e) institutional
strengthening.
Benefits:              The main project benefits would include: (a) increased on-
farm productivity and family incomes and improved welfare;
(b) improved and more cost-effective agricultural services;
(c) improvement in the credit-worthiness of subsistence
farmers; and (d) better targeting of public services
including improvement of services to women farmers.



-2-
Risks:                The principaL project risks would be: (a) the potential
for political over-eagerness to expand programs to
address rural povsrty, threatening INDAP's .operational
capability; and (b) reluctance of commercial credit
market to extend credit to graduates of INDAP program.
Riolk (a) is already being minimized by a generalized
fiscal austerity, acting as a break on imprudent
expansion of INDAP programs. As regards risk (b), the
project would attempt to change commercial credit
market attitudes by (i) pilot testing various options
proposed by the study on improving incentives for
private intermediaries; and (ii) hy improving subloan
portfolio management to clarify each client's payment
record.

It.  TII AGRICI,LTURA. SECTOR
Agricu1ture in the Ec_ono y
2.01        Since the mid-1960s, t'a agricultural sector of Chile has
experi.enced an extended period of growth, onily briefly interrupted by the
turmoil of the early 19708 and macroeconomic imbalances in the early 1980s.
Since 1985, agriculture has become a dynamic and increasingly vital sector of
the economy, growing at a rate of about 52 per annum. The cector-includI:ng
forestry and fisheries--currently generates about 9% of GDP, employs 18% of
the labor force and accounts for over one-third of imerchandise exports.
2.02        Recent PI2i,L En _ ronment.  Macroeconomic and trade policy have a
major influence on the performance of the sector, which produces predominantly
tradable commodities, and recent growth can be attributed largely to sustained
trade liberalization policies and economic stability since 1985. Trade reform
has been complemented by the deregulation of factor markets and a series of
innovative sectoral measures which have promoted the efficient allocation of
resources to and within agriculture. With a relatively small domestic markret,
exports have accounted for most of the growth. Led by fresh fruit, forest and
fish products, the value of exports relative to agricultural value-added in-
creaced from less than 5% in 1965 to 20% in 1982 and over 80% during
1988/1990. The sector's current 35% share of foreign exchange earnings
rerrcsents a major increase from 8% in 1985 and 6% in 1973. Import-competing
crops also responded and the country is now self-sufficient in wheat and
sugar. Food imports as a share of total imports have declined from 20% in
1965 to 4% in 1988/1990.
2.03        Recent achievements need to be consolidated into the 1990s and
their sustainability ensured. Heavy investments in fruit orchards need
reliable water supplies and sanitary protection. Unsustainable production
practices in the forest and fisherlies subsectors that threaten the resource
endowments on which they depend require a significant tightening of the
regulation and management of natural resources. Also, the potential of the
small farm sector has not been tapped fully, and rural poverty persists.
Attaining these objectives will require few significant changes in policy.
The fundamental requirements remain: (a) sustained relative openness in goods
markets effected through liberalized trade policies and fiscal stringency to
support a favorable real exchange rate; (b) a stable and favorable investment
climate with consistent "rules of the game"; (c) continuing efforts to develop
and deregulate capital, land, and labor markets; and (d) development and
dissemination of small-farmer technology, complemented by credit, to enable
small farm development.
The Small Farm Sector
2.04        An Overview.  The rural population accounts for about 17% of
Chile's 12.8 million inhabitants. Current estimates by the Agricultural. De-
velopment Institute (INDAP) divids the farm population into three major cat-
egories:
(a)   an estimated 33,000 farmers who own and operate modern, medium-
size and larger farm enterprises, accounting for about 53% of the
agricultural area and generating 70% of marketed output;

(b)   about 148,000 farm familins with 5-12. BIHN/, coTmprising mostly
agrariarn reform becucfLciarieo and small traditional proprietors,
who contro). 452 of thio agrIcultural aroa nnd produce most of the

(a)   245,000 fauilleta  ineluding 105,000 smaLl holders (;xxinifundic;ltn)
with le.ees than 5 BIIl and 1-22 of the aratb.te area and 140,000 other
rural familieo whoso rmain sources of income are oharecropping and
wage income from rural labor.
2.05        Stooll fiamere (those in grotips (b) and (c) above)23  play art
important role in the national and regional oconomi:Le of Chliloe. They account
for somO 53% of the total area allocatecl to vegetablec aso well as one-third of
the area under cereals, wine grapes and natural pasture.  A limited national
agricultural and livestock survey of 1986/87 demonstrated that some 962 of
small farmers are located between the Rio Elqui and Chilo6, with a marked
concentration between Ruble and Chilo6, i.e., between Regions VIII and X (see
Map IBRD 22694). Thi.s same study revoaled th&: while the majority of small
farmers are situated in marginal, dry coastal areas and Andean foothills, over
one-third are located in the fertile, irrigated lands of the Central Valley.
These small Central Valley producers generally have better resources and
greater commercial potential than those in the coastal. areas and foothills
and, therefore, comprise the bulk of families in category (b) above.
2.06        Dependence on family labor distinguishes shurply the smaller from
the larger producer. While all family members work, women and younger family
members are generally unpaid, but adult male members receive some form of
payment either in-kind or in-cash. This dependence on family labor explains
partially the ability of small farmers to withstand economic adversity and has
also proved invaluable in sustaining production during seasonal periods when
male family members migrate either within Chile or to other parts of the
Southern Cone to find seasonal work, eog., fruit-picking, fish-farming and
logging within Chile, and sheep-sheAring in Argentina.
2.07        Conatraints to Growth.  Apart from the small farmers living in the
fertile Central Valley, the soil and climatic conditions in areas of small
farmer concentration are poor. Novertheless, the higher yields of larger
farmers in these more marginal areas indicate a potential for higher
productivity among small farmers. While medium- and large-scale agricultural
entrepreneurs have been able to invest in new plant varietles, pesticides,
fertilizers and modern machinery generally associated with modern technology,
small producers often have lacked such access owing to: (a) the almost
complete absence in Chile of research end development of techmology
appropriate to their special conditions (e.g., area-specific, robust crop
varieties, crop/livestock/for'stry farming systems, appropriate tools,
environmentally sound tillage methods, simple preservation techniques);
(b) lack resources at the farm leve'l to pay for available technology (e.g.,
certified seed, fertilizers); and (c) resource constraints on public technical

2.08        In addition, small producers lack basic servico infrastructureo
including collection, storages processing and marketing facilities. 'The
situation is further exacerbated by poor secondary roads, especially from

Public Sector Assistance Pr   ms
2.09        The A_riulj turalDove elen t Insttute (INDAP) was created in 1962
to provide credit and free technical assistance to small producers.  It is an
agency within the Ministry of Agriculture (MAG) mandated to increase small
farmer productivity, bring them into the national economic mainstream whero
possible and improve thelr general living standards (see Annexes 2.1 and 8).
I1DAP is headed by a National Director operating through a National D:lr-.
e,:torate, 12 Regional Directorates and 74 Area agencies. Its main responsi-
billties are: (a) technical assistance (technology trnsfer); (b) com-
plementary investment and working capital financing; and (c) incorporation of
smnall farmers into the National Plan for Rural Development (para. 2.10).
I.IDAP beneficiaries oust satisfy the following qualifications: (i) farm lees
than 12 BIH; (ii) have total assets les than 3,500 UF (US$73,500);
(iii) obtain income principally from agricultural pursuits whatever the land
tenancy arrangements; and (iv) exhibit potential to improve agricultural
performance. INDAP currently hae a staff of 970 made up of 70 manag-
efs/directors, 245 professionals, 290 tech:nicians and 365 support staff,
2.10        In 1986, an Inter-Ministerial Cocmisoion for Rural Developmont wan
e3tablished to formulate and unify public policy for improving the quality of
life for the Chilean rural sector. The Commission recommended the
iuiplementatioa of a National Plan for Rural Development (Plan), representing a
multi-faceted attack on rural poverty and on the wide divergence of social
indicators between rural and urban areas. With its Plan, the Commission
sought: (a) to boost income-generating capacity by modernizing agriculture
through more effective technology transfer, by upgrading the entrepreneurial
capability of producers, by improving marketing potential, and by issuing land
titles; and (b) to enhance the quality of life in rural areas by expanding and
making more efficient social programs in the areas of education, health and
housing. Launched in early 1987, the Plan is administered at the national,
regional and provincial levels by participant-agencies, but actual
implementation, and partial but limited financing, are the responsibility of
local communities.
2.11        Technolov Transfer.  After a aerious curtailment of its acti-
vities in the 1970s, INDAP introduced a pilot technical assistance program in
1982, using subsidized private consulting services in an area south of
S.antiago.  In 1983/84, its program was oxpanded to cover 13,140 beneficiarine



nationwide. Until 1967/88, INDAt"s program of technology transfer grew
modestly, principally reflecting renource limitations and the multi.-year
nature of benafi.::iary participav.ioun  INOAP's program was llmited initially to

2.12        Once the Plan was latnched, INDAP's program of technology transfer
was enlarged and broadenecl.  The prog,ram for better-endowed small producers
(nonm fundistan) was enlarged by one-third in 198i/88, servicing about
18,600 farmers (Proglemoa Transferencia Tocnol          i v _t__aLPT_ L)_
Furthermore, in 1987/88 INDAP began a special program of technical assistance
geared to the formerly neglected, smaller and poorer segment of tho farming
population (Ergramade Trannferoncla Tecnol6pg!c    ica__ PTTBP)   PTTI
beneficiaries are small, marginal producers (mjnifundista.) considered to have
inadequate resources to achieve self-sufficiency from on-farm purnuito, in
many cases ever after PTTB participation. Modelled on PTTI, PTTB baoically
sought to improve the quality of life of participating families through
medium-term technical assistance orressing improved use of available on-farm
resources and siuple methods to upgrade family healthi (i.e.* diet, hygiene,
water treatment, awarenoss of social sorvicess). In 1Q87/88, 6,900 familias
participated in PTTB, a number which increased slightly to 7,000 in 1988/89.
PTTB has generally been considered as having a aclial emphasis and as being a
link with other social interventions under the Plat. thoughl INDAP's current
administration is attempting to strengthen the program's productive side. In
contrast, 1'TTI focused traditionally on the productive/commercial development
of participants, with socia". developmeut ari.sing ao a secondary benefit.
2.13        In 1989/90, the number of PTTI participants was cut to about
16,000, or by about 14%, to provide greater resources for PTTB which was
increased to about 11,000 femilies in the same year.  Farmers eliminated from
PTTI were offered tec-hnical assistance in the form of technical meetings and
field days; however, only about 875 such producers opted to participate !n
these less intensive activities. During 199P'91, the number of PTTI
beneficiaries remaine, stable at 16,000, but PTTB increased to about 17,900.
2.14        Under its PTTI and PTTB programs, INDAP has developed a fairly
efficient model of agricultural extension which minimizes public bureaucracy
and which includes effective controls to ensure quality. Its staff appear
capabla and conversant with field-related matters. Private firms contracted
to work in PTTI and PTTB appear well-qualified, enthusiastic and dedicated.
The use of local firms, with staff from the same areas in which they work,
increases understanding of local conditions and chances for acceptance, which
is particularly important for PTTB. INDAP's system of decentralization
fosters decision-making at the local level, which is conducive to interaction
with other agencies participating in the Plan--especially important in view of
the social goals of PTTB. Quantitative results confirm the success of the
INDAP model of technical assistance, complemented with on-farm credit
(Annex 3 ?.
2.15        The Institute of Agricultural Research (INIA) is the primary
public agency charged with agricultural and livestock research and its
application to the Chilean agricultural sector. Until recently, INIA
concentrated on the needs of medium and large producers, reflecting the
inability of small farmers to pay for INIA services and a lack of acceptance
in INIA that specialized, small-producer technology was needed. INIA,



7 -
howevor, is required under an ongoing ID-fin.anced Technology Generation and
Transfer Project (Ln. 171-EC-CH) to estab3ioh special units for the
demonstration of appropriate technology to groups of INDAP beneficiaries
(Centers of Technological Change and Technology Transfer (CATTe)). Results
suggest that technology exhibited at these centore has lacked sm813 farmer
focus and generally has been beyond the finaciiial capacity of small farmers to
adopt. The situation is improving rapidly, however, as the new Government hae
modified INIA's mandate to include explicitly small farmer technology require-
ments. Additionally, INIA, INDAP and firms implementing INDAP's technology
transfer programs are now collaborating to formulate regional strategies for
technology development and transfer. A new IDB loan, signed on
Marchl 13, 1992, is is expected to finance the generation of amall-farmnr
technology by INIA. At neaotiations, the Government agreed to support such
research services.
2.16        As regards PTTB, extension workers invest considerable time and
effort to win the trust of farmers who can ill-afford to risk scarce resources
on innovation. For that reason, PTTB is built chiefly on better use of exist-
ing resources and seeks to minimize risks. Its relatively simple
technological improvements--e.g., organic kitchen gardens, raised garden beds,
diet diversification, confined poultry raising, crop rotations--appear to be
improving the quality of life for beneficiaries. In addition, PTTB extension
workers are able to raise awareness of other available social programs--e.g.,
health services, housing subsidies, and education programs. INDAP's
administration is exploring group activities/small-scale enterprises for
smaller farmers as a way of omircoming their limited "individual" production
and marketing capabilities.
2.17        Credit.  In addition to technical assistance, INDAP provides its
clientele with credit to finance working capital and productive investments.

individual borrowers (chiefly for annual crop production) constituted about

involves stiffer terms.

2.19        INDAP's Financial Condition.  The main financial issue rea,Arding
IND"A is iIts level of oubloan arrears. At ond-1990, INDAP quotod P cu-zrellt
level of arrears of 312.  A consultant study9 completed in August 1991,
confirmed this level of arrearo.  Both the Government (t:hrough the. 4inistry of
Finance) and INDAP's new management have acknowledged t]lo severity of the
problem and consider the situation to be unacceptable.  They have discussed
the budgetary implication of INDAP9s operations under the project in great
detail and have arrived at a tight plan of financing which requires a subloan
collection rate of 90% by fiscal year 1995. Aty subloan collection rate lowCer
than this would imply a smaller INDAP lending operation and would reiflect:
adversely on its management.  In order to comply with the Government's target,
and improve the collection rate, INDAP has decided to limit all the growth in
its credit programs to minifundistao.  Tnese clients have a better record of
repaying loans than INDAY's traditional clients (non-minifundiatas) since
(a) they borrow amall amounts (less than 30 UFs or around US$600 for short-
term loans and less than 100 UFa for :Long-term loans); (b) they usually
augment their income with non-farm activities, thereby reducing fluctuations
in their income; anid (c) they are more inclined to establish a credit record
to gain access to commercial credit. During appralsal, it was determined that
the subloan collection rate on a representative sample of short-term loans,
made in 1989 (payments received in 1989 and 2990) was above 81%.  However, the
collection rate on loans below 30 UPs from the same sampled areas was over
872. Thus it pays for INDAP to focus on minifundiptas, (Similar data on
loans made during and after 1990 and on long-term loans, are not yet available
because payments are still being made.) Further improvement in the collection
rate is expected to coxme from: (a) the introduction of monitorable indicators;
(b) annual and mid-ter-m reviews; (a) adoption of an improved system of arrears
accounting to identify more clearly subloans at risk and signal more rapidly
the need for remedial action by INDAP; and (d) adoption of a new credit
approval system which would include a credit ratir'Z system to help prevent
lending to clients with poor credit histories. Safeguards for the Bank
regarding portfolio management are discussed in paragraph 4.19.
2.20        Rural Women and Youth.  'he socio-economic impact of rural women
in Chile in recent year3 has had only sporadic study and is not
well-understood.  Women's participation in the economically active rural
population increased dramatically in the 1980s, and there is a strong
association between rural poverty alleviation and women's work in agricultural
production and processing. Evidence suggests that women are earning en
increasing proportion of the family income and in a growing number of cases
are now the principal breadwiniers.  It is estimated conservatively that some
15% of poor rural households in Chile are hestded by womens However, until now,
economic planners, legislators and rural development agencies haye tended tc
reinforce women's domestic role rather than their economic potential. For
oxample, many assistance programs have had a "head of household" criterion
which has in practice excluded women; a narrow definition, of what constitute
"productive activities" has caused womenls work to be discourted; and cultural
bias has caueed technical training to be directed almost exclusively to men.



- 10
In INDAP, the consequence of such policies has been:  (a) an exten ion system
which focussed narrowly on women's traditional role as food-growcrs,
carepgivers and maintainers of basic family living standardas, in marked
contrast to the more complex, productive reality of rural women's lives; and
(b) credit programs whiich have tended to give uriority to male borrowers, on
the basis of the "head of household" and "productive activities" criteria, as
.:cll ac collateral requircmenta that largely exclud' women.
2.21        Howover, working with the newly-entablished National Women's
Service (SERNAM), whose mandate is to ensure the incorporation of gender
perspectives into ministerial planning, and co:tllborating with the major NGOs,
wbO.rh hlrtw  conv:iderable experience working with rural women, INDAP is
attempting to address a range of issues affecting rural women through a
gradual. re-orientation of both its extension and credit programs. New
programs are still in their infancy, but merit full support to devel2e
momentum and sustainability.  The program for women under the propoi ed project
is discussed in detail in paras. 4.22 and 4.23,
2.22        The average age of INDAP beneficiary heads of household is 53.5
years. Poor rural youth are estimated to number 603,000 persons, the majority
of whom are considerably better-educated than their parents, but who
navertheless face an uncertain future. The dilemma for planners is whether to
encourage youth to remain on the land or provide incentives for a fUtulre
elsewhere (training, job creation strategies). Many small farms cam._t
support more than one family and migration to urban areas is inevitable. In
response to a growing perception that better-educated rural youth are not only
promising subjects for more complex technical training, but can be a central
element in the rural development strategy, INDAP is already implementing a
pilot program for the 18-30 year age cohort called PTT Fomento (PTTF)v
focussed initially in the Metropolitan Region. INDAP contracts local
agronomists to provide a high-intensity technology transfer of 30 visits per
year to groups averaging five members, who cultivate selected strains of
tomatoes under "hothouse" conditions (i.e., under plastic), for sale in
Regional centers in the off season. The PTT includes marketing, accounting
and decision making. INDAP is working with 30 groups and hopes to expand this
to 200 by the end of 1992, moving into carefully-selected micro-regions and
other forms of activities, including milk processing and flowers. Groups
would be fully-kraduated after two years' participrtion.
2.23        There is at present no special budget for this important program.
Its survival depends largely on the goodwill of Regional Directoro of INDAP.
There is also a need to find a mechanism to connect this program into the
mainstream of IIDAP credit, since youth rarely have the traditional forms of
collateral needed to go beyond INDAP's Cr6dito Bisico. Further, there are as
yet no women in PTTF, due, in part, to cultural perceptions about appropriate
group activities for unmarried females. INDAP is, accordingly, now targeting
young married couples. (For further details on rural women and youth, see the
Appendix to Annex 3).

Pr ivate Assistance Trograms
2.29        Numerous private agencies are engaged in rural development
activities in Chile.  These non-governmental organizations (NG0s), numbering
about 610 arose to fill a vacuum created with the demise in the mid- and late-
1970s of public programs to assist small farmers. Many NGOs receive financial
assistance from external sources, some channelled through religious
organizations. The principal target group of NGOs has been and remains the
smallest farmers and poorest of the rural population (including indigenous
people and beneficiaries of Chile's agrarian reform program). As public
programs were broadened in the 1980s to include not only small farmers with
commercial potential (PTTI) but also the poorest of the small farmer
population (PTTB), NGOs have diversified, incorporating small producers with
commercial potential into their scope of activity. NGO projects generally
encompass both productive and social aspects, with individual emphasis
depending on the philosophical orientation and the staffing of each NGO.
Beneficiaries of NGO programs of direct assistance are estimated at about
15,000 families (for details see Annex 3).
Government's Strateav
2.30        The Government's program for agriculture is outlined in Annex 1.
Inter alia, this gives priority to raising the productive performance, incomes
and living standards of the rural poor. In this context, INDAP's role is
fundamental, as outlined in its strategic plan in Annex 2.1. MBN's strategic
plan, as outlined in Annex 2.2, also plays a vital part in furthering the
Government's socio-economic agenda.
III.  BANK INVOLVEMENT AND LESSONS LEARNED
Bank Involvement in the Sector
3.01        The Bank's experience with agricultural and rural development
(ARD) lending, worldwide and in Chile, has spurred debate on the role and
appropriate direction of such lending into the 19909. While there have been
significant variations in performance betwaen Regions and subsectors, only
some 602 of completed ARD projects have been evaluated as satisfactory.V'
Among the most successful have been agricultural research and extension
projects, infrastructure projects and perennial crops. Area development
projects, on the other hand, especially inte3rated ARD operations, have fallen
far short of their earlier promise chiefly because expectations were too high,i
Z/ The most favorable impaot of investments In ARD projects hue been In Asila Those judged unsatfactory were reported chiefly
In two Regions. ArlIo and to a leser extent, Ltin America and the Calbbean.



- 13 -
not because the concept was inherently wrong. In general, they were ovorly
ambitious in scope and ncale, required exceptional leadership and coiuW>:111tewnt,
and were difficult to suotainO. More specifically, inadequate attnnttlon uiao
paid to appropriate analysis of the existing agricultural situation,
institutional chortcomings  beneficiary involvement, realistic coordInating
arrangements and implementation schedules, and monitoring and evaluation0
Unsatisfactory performance was by no means always design-related. Inadequate
macroeconomic and trade policies eJere probably the main causes for the poor
performance of maniy agricultural projects in the LAC Region in the 1980s0.R
In addition, limited infrastructure construction and maintenance, nnd
inadequate technical services have caused the demise of otherwise well-
designed projects.1'
3.02        The Bank's extensive ARV lending experience has thus producod
important, specific lessons which have been instructional in designing the new7
project in Chile. Prominent among the lessons of thiu lending is that the
Bank should move away from the generalized global strategy of ARD projects
which are too comprehensive to a more phased, structured and selective
approach, focussing on a limited number of subsectors considered essential  for
small farmer development. As described in para. 4.04, the proposed project in
Chile is, in essence, a modified RD operation with a tighter focus on four
critical areas--technical assistance, credit, land titling and rural
communications (an innovation which sector work demonstrates could have broaa
benefits, but which has few precedents in Bank ARD lending)--supported by
institutional strengthening designed to promote efficiency and sustainability.
3.03        Technical Assistance.  Agricultural extension has comprised a
fairly low proportion of ARD lending but has a vital role in increasing the
effectiveness of other interventions, including irrigation and new
technologies. Analysis shows that achieving the right balance between
established norms and flexibility enhances the chances for its successful
introduction and implementation, while it is essential that a realistic
assessment be made of the time-frame needed to aiter the scale and methodology
of existing extension systems or to establish new ones. The lessons of
effective extension demonstrate the need for: (a) a favorable policy
environment offering adequate incentives to producers; (b) appropriate
technology and its well-designed liakage to extension; (c) well-trained middle
management and subject matter specialists; (d) firm official commitment to
extension in principle, since the observable relationship between the large
investment and farmer productivity is not always obvious; and (e) a minimum of
non-extension tasks burdening technicians. Lastly, efforts to address
directly the needs of women through extension are having impact in some areaf,
but the LAC Region lags and needs special attention..'
3.04        Agricultural Credit.  The Bank's agricultural credit lending
between 1982-1989 mostly fell short of its objectives because it was a supply-
led approach, failing to correct major defects in the institutional
agricultural credit systems of borrowing countries. Because of inadequate
rigor in subloan administration, low and frequently negative interest rates

operationo.  Issues such as tho reliability of its subloan arrears
data, the absence of a suitable intenial control and auditing
systerm, and a mechanism for the transparentl formulation of
subsidies, have been cause for Bank concern since the early 1980s.
F1urther, INDAP's poor systemi of portfolio accounting acts as a
deterrent to the acceptance of INDAP clientele by the commercial
banking system, hindering their graduation from, and perpetuating
the need for, the subsidized systetm;
(b)      INDAP needs to design e imechanism for graduating extene.on
clientele out of its program, especially since its cost recovery
objectives remain modest, eveni for the better-off small farmers,
and threateni INDAP's longer-termy ability to expand the program;
and
(c)      Successful agricultural credit lending requires a favorable
scatoral environment, an}1d ouch projects/components should be
sufficiently flexible in design to be reeponsive to changes in
credit demand, which may result from shifting  economic
cilrcumtcAnces alid policies.

and services to improve overall welfare; (b) alleviating povertyl
(c) strengthening the institutional capacity of public sector agencies to
carry out their respective roles more effectively; (d) improving the natiorn's
capacity to protect its natural resource-based economy against enviroLnmental

recommendations made in the 1990 Agricultural Sector Review which advocat:ed,
inter alis, selective, but fiscally prudent growth in small farmer
initiatives, as well as complementary programs to generate small producer
technology and reactivate land titling, particularly in rural areas. The
principal Bank role would be: (i) to sapport a manageable expansion of INDAP's
technical assistance and credit operations, and to do so in an efficient and
cost-effective manner; (ii) to encourage INDAP to see its credit operations as
a temporary association with individual farmers, during which the principal
objective would be to equip each farmer to meet the requirements of, and
graduate to, the private banking system as soon as possible; (iii) to
encourage INDAP to manage its subloan portfolio based on sound business
principles, including, in particular, debt collections; and (iv) to help
nccelerate land titling.
B.  Project Obiectives and Descliption
4.03        The proposed project would: (a) overcome constraints to increased
small-farmer productivity and incomes and improved well-being; (b) enhance the
institutional capacity and effectiveness of INDAP and MBN; and (c) foster
small-farmer access to commercial markets for their products and factors of
production, and to publicly,sponsored agricultural development programs.
4.04        To accomplish t'hese objectives the project would primarily focus
on the poorest small farmers (minifundistas, para. 2.04) and would include:
(a) teclnology transfer, involving both farm-oriented extension and home-
centered assistance (family kitchen gardens, water treatment, improved diets
and cooking methods, awareness of education and health services); (b) credit
for working capital (e.g., seed, fertilizers) and investment (e.g., fencing,
reproductive and draft animals, orchard establishment, implements, on-farm
storage), consistent with technology transfer initiatives; (c) a rural
communication system to enable better diseemination of information related to
small farmer production and rural social services; (d) land titling in rural
areas; and (e) institutional strengthening. The project would be implemented
over a five-year period and would be nationwide in scope. To ensure that the
project is consistent with Government and Banik objectives, prior to
negotiations MAG, INDAP and MBN submitted acceptable statements of
policy/3trategic plans (as per drafts in Document 1 of Techniical File, and
Annexes 2.1 and 2.2).
4.05        INDAP's well-designed system of technology transfer would be the
core component of the new operation. The project would incorporate the
following innovations: (a) greater targeting of appropriate technology toward
the poorest small farmers; (b) emphasis on graduation from both technology
transfer and credit programs; (c) sound subloan portfolio accounting and
remedial measures to improve subloan collections; (d) more use of NGOs and
exioting farmer organizations in the technology transfer process; (e) enhanced
monitoring and evaluation; (f) more use of advanced audio-visual techniques in
oxtcno4.on; (g) specialized environmental traininlg for extension workers; (h) a
iiex fogcs on wamen as targets of farm-oriented technical aus,stance; and
(j) gOvotov  a.nticipation by fatmer orgaui2atiowl  vu benefiGianou.

LVRJLqcqt 2itlz_mentation
4.06        The technology transfer and complemeYltary credit components of the
project would be implemented by INDAP (paras. 4.08 to 4.24) for which an
institutional development program would bo carried out to enhance administra-
tion and subloan portfolio management (par&. 4.25). In addition, institutional
improvements would include enhancements to INDAP's information systems and a
better monitoring and evaluation capability. The rural communication system
would be carried out under INDAP guidance by the Agricultural Communications
Foundation (FUCOA) located within HAG.
4.07        MBN would implement tho land titling component (para. 4.26),
following its proven syotem of contracting private firms for cadastral and
legal work. HBN's administrative and supervisory capacity would be enhanced
through a larger motor pool to enable better supervision, selective technical
assistance and a computerized information system to speed up overall
processing at the central and regional levels, including title record-keeping
and accounting (para. 4.27).
C. Detailed Prolect Features
INDAP Exected Coumonents
4.08        TechnoloAvt Transfer  P1T) (Annex 3) (US$ 84.0 million, 37.0% of
base costs). The overall objective of the technology transfer component would
be to increase the on-farm productivity, income and well-being of about 84,000
small farmers throughout Chile. The project would build on the lessons
learned from Loan 2481-CH by:
(a)      placing greater emphasis on minifundistas--the numbo. of program
beneficiaries who are minifundistas would grow four-fold to
68,000--and gradually phasing out subsidized extension support to
non-minifundista families (ex-PTTI);
(b)      improving the quality of extension through better training of
personnel, providing increased incentives to good performers,
greater relevance and evaluation of technical packages generated
by INIA research, and increasing the involvement of NGO and
agroindustry in planning, training and marketing;
(c)      enhancing efficiencies in service delivery through the adoption of
an integrated rural communication system, including audio-visual
techniques in group sessions;
(d)      rewarding excellence in the performance of field extension;
(e)      strengthening monitoring and evaluation to ensure effective
planning and efficient delivery systems;
(£)      increasing the involvement of existing rural community
organizations as catalysts for change; and
(g)      focussing greater efforts on the untapped potential of rural women
and rural youth.

4.14        Improved cost affic:ivncioo expucted under thu project are ohown in
Toble 4.1.  The number of farm fi¼v.lTloo contactod per extvnaion agelnt would
increase from 66 pre-projoet to /2 in Phase 1. and 90 in Phase U1. As a
rooult, the unit cost of providing theuo s.oivNicns would declone oubsotantially
from US$240 per fami:ly to US$140.
4.15        Bly July 1, 1994, an indepondent mid-term review of the project
would be completed. Thin wouldt (a) evalunte overall progreso; (b) determine
tho suutability of graduating some farmers from Phase I; and (c) set targets
for coverage for tho socond half of the project implementation period.  On the
Itint poinit, r,ince the project would focus heavily on improving the quality of
oxtension, INDAP would have to resist the temptation of embarking upon a
strategy of maximizing coverage before qualitative gai:nu have been
consolidated.  This focus on quality would improve tho prospects of long-term
sustainability of INDAP's extension program and would enoure that its services
produce maximum impact at the level of each farm family. Too rapid a build-up
in coverage would risk overextending field exteaoion personnel.  Such a
strategy would lead to a focus on attracting new farmers into the program
rather than building a working relationship which would help farmers better
exploit their available resources, thereby permitting graduation to higher
levels of self reliance. The proposed project atrategy would control
unsustainable administrative cost increases associated with a rapid build-up
in field staff required to service large numbers of farm families early in the
implementation period. As farmers graduate from the program, this will permit
new farners to join without requiring aniy further build up of IXDAP staff.
4.16        The project would strengthen INDAP management's capr-bility to
develop long-term development strategies for the small fa-mer sector, with
targets and monitorable indicators. In addition to the mid-term review, there
would be an annual system of "physical audito" carried out by independent
agencies (universities or conoultants) to asoist INDAP mianagement further in
strategic planning. To carry out the extension program, INDAP would establish
17 new area offices, bringing the total to 91 in 13 regions. In addition,
65 temporary offices ("oficinas de atenci6n") wousld be set up in isolated
areas where extension staff would operate on a limited basis on a pre-
established schedule.  Th; complement of field staff would increase by about
395 (about 40%), of which 89 would be secretaries for area offices, since only
two area offices currently have secretaries.  This 1nerease is consistent with
an efficient program. The estimated base cost of the teclnology transfer
component is about US$84.0 million equivalent, of which US$81.4 million Would
be for field activities and US$2.6 million for training ancl technical support.
4.17       Credit (US$116.2 million, 51.2% of base coots).  A key service to
complement technology transfer would be short-term credit for worlking capital
and term credit for on-farm and simple agroindustrial investments. About
100,000 farm families (more than 80% minifundistas) would receive credit under
the project, including INDAP'. teclmo10gy transfer beneficiaries and client.
obtaining technical assistance from NGOs, agroinduotries, farmer groups and
INDAP on a less formal basis. This linkage between INDAP credit and INDAP
technical assistance would increase from pre-project lovels of about 20%, to
about 74% by year 5 of the project.  INDAP would also encourage credit to
qualified farmer organizations for simplo storage and Agri   ropoceo ng; a
notable success in this regard under Loan 2481-CH ha& been subloana for milk
collection and chill1ing facilities,

the range of finanicial product,e nad the range of ftianc:Lel vb viceoO available

to small farmers must increase. Helping small farmers meet the requirements
of the commercial banking sector for credit has become the guiding prirciplo
of INDAP's involvement in credit. Thus, INDAP's role would be that of
facilitator, preparing small farmers for graduation into the commercial
banking sector.  However, incentives will have to be developed to stimulate
the commercial banking sector to accept such small farmer clients whom they
currently classify as high risk. In this context, an independent otudy would
be completed in 1994 by INDAP, under the supervision of the Ministry of
Finance, with input from the banking sector, INDAP, universities, NGOs, and
private consultants, to study such incentives. However, subsidi.s through
interest rates and subloan guarantees would not be acceptable. The goal would
be to develop a series of options to stimulate greater banking sector
involvement which would then be pilot-tested under the direction of the
Ministry of Finance in 1993-94. During neaotiation , assurances were obtained
that INDAP, under the supervision of the Ministry of Finance, would complete
six months after the date of effectiveness, a study on improving the
incentives for private intermediaries to lend to farmers graduating fromi the
INDAP credit program. By December 31, 1994, INDAP would complete
implementation of pilot schemes to test the viability of the variouu optiono
proposed. The terms of reference (TOR) for the study were given to the Bank
for review and approval during negotiations.
4.19      INDAP vould make subloans under terms and conditions included in
General Credit Regulations (Reglamento de Cr6dito), Agreements were obtained
prior to nezotiations on such regulations and supporting administrative
directives (as per draft in Document 2 of Technical File). Ratification by
INDAP/MAG of agreed Credit Regulations would be a condition of effectiveness.
Moreover, to improve INDAP's subloan portfolio performance (see paras. 2,19),
agreement was reached at neaotiations that INDAP's collection rate (the
amount, principal and interest, due and collected in each fiscal year over the
amount due in the same period) for all subloans disbursed after January 1,
1992 would be no lower than 85% in Fiscal Year 1992, and would rise to 88% in
Fiscal Year 1994, and to 90% in Fiscal Year 1995, except subloans for
officially-declared national emergency relief which would be limited, in the
case of INDAP, to a maximum of 10% of subloans disbursed in that Fiscal Year.
In order to improve its track record in portfolio management and reach these
targets, IIDAP would implement the following reforms:
(a) Credit Information Hanaeement. The computerized Credit Information
System (CREDAP) developed under Ln. 2481-Ch enables better credit
administration and better monitoring and evaluation of the end-uses
of credit. While the CREDAP system is already operational, the
Bank will evaluate its capabilities and readiness prior to
effectiveness. The accounting information system would indicate:
(i) the collection rates of subloans disbursed in any particular
period; (iiJ the (ex-post) subsidy due to losses from recovery or,
these subloans; and (iii) the aging of arrears of the portfolio.
The above data would be sub-divided by different subloan
characteristics such as point of origin, type of loan, gender of
recipient etc., to the point that INDAP management and the Govern-
ment will monitor continuously the health of INDAP'. lending opera-
tions and will identify quickly individual subloans at risk for
follow-up action. Moreover, to improve the use of personnel, the
new system would also generate every month a list of accounte for
which amounts would fali due in that month. A proposed system for
generating this list, developed by consultants financed under Ln,
2481-CH, has been reviewed and found acceptable to the Bank.

4,24      Rural Co5mmnications (US$2.2 million, 1.02 of base costs).  To
increase the effectiveness of INDAP's technology transfer program and to
disseminate information about productive schemes and social services available
in rural areas, the project would include an integrated communication system.
Television and radio programs would be used to: (a) educate the general
public about the rural sector's problems and the importance of its
development; (b) provide farmers with appropriate information as a basis for
decision-making; and (c) mobilize the rural populace for development action
and innovation. Although INDAP would initially finance rural TV and radio
programs, the objective would be to stimulate greater private sector
responsibility for these productions. The largest subcomponent of the rural
communications strategy involves audio-visual and print media, which would be
used to: (i) enhance the capability of extension activities; and (ii) collect
and share information between farmers, farm families and technicians about
development problems and options, and technologies for resolving them. The
production of videos would be carried out by the private sector and extension
consultants would be expected to own their own projection equipment.
Communication activities would be implemented by a small team of professionals
located in FUCOA (which is in MAG). Most of the production and research work
would be contracted out to private firms, NGOs and "peasant correspondents".
Given the lack of experience in FUCOA and INDAP in the systematic application
of communiication to rural development, about 51 man-months of technical
assistance would be provided to launch the component. Project-specific
administration etaff would include a rural communications specialist and an
instructional materials specialist. (For further details see Document 3 of
Technical File).

Accounts and Audits
5.07      The PCNU would have overall responsibility for project
coordination. Executing agencioo would maintain separate accotnts to record
their expenditures under the project. All such agencies would maintain
ceparate project accountn and records for each project compoxnent, consolidato
them into the project accounts and prepare Statements of Expenditures (SOEs).
These SORs would be presented to the Treasury for reimbursement out of the
project'o special account (para. 4.31). Supporting documentation provided by
implementing agencies would be maintained by the respective agencies and be
made available to vioiting Bank misaions and independent auditors, who at
thoir discretion would make direct vioits to executing agencieo for the
purpose of the overall audit.
5.08      The Government would require INDAP and MBN to use a standard set of
operating, administrative and accounting procedures. Moreover, project
accounts, including the special account, SOEs and the financial statements of
INDAP and MBN would be audited annually in accordance with appropriate
auditing principles conointently applied by independent auditors acceptable to
the Bank according to terms-of-reference approved by the Bank. During
negotiations, the Government agreed to the above accounting and auditing
conditions.
Ret -nig
5.09      Monitoring of progress on implementation would be carried out by
the respective implementing agency (INDAP, MBN or FUCOA); however, mid-term
and ex- post evaluation of the impact of the project would be carried out by
consultants contracted by the PCMU. A preliminary, agency-led evaluation
would be carried out at the end of year 1 to identify areas in need of
immediate adjustment.  During  gotiations, the Government provided assurances
that it would: (a) send semi-annual progress reports to the Bank not later
than two montlhs after the end of each reporting period; (b) furnish yearly
plans of action of INDAP and MBN to the Bank by October 31st of the previous
year, starting October 31, 1992; and (c) employ consultanto by April 1, 1994
and April 1, 1996 to carry out an indepth review of the progress made in the
implementation of the project, with reports sent to the Bank not later than
June 15, 1994 and June 30, 1996, respectively.
VI. PROJECT BENEFITS, RISKS, AND ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT
Benefits
6.01      An economic rate of return has not been calculated due to the
service and institutional development nature of the non-credit components.
However, the project would have a major impact on the quality of life, income
and legal security (titling) of a significant proportion of the low income,
small farmer population of Chile. Further details on the target population
are gi'yen in Annex 3, Table 1.
6.02      While it is difficult to be precise, broad-based and substantial
increases in family well-being are expected as a result of the proposed
project.  The technology transfer program would benefit about 84,000
smallholder families at full.development, and INDAP's credit program would
benefit about 100,000 farm families (about 33Z and 402, respectively, of the
small farmers in Chile). The project would have an added benefit of targeting
rural women in these farm families, reinforcing Government's new initiative to
recognize the important role of woraen in the Chilean economy. The land



_ 33 -
titling component would benefit about 43,500 families thereby praviding
security of land holding and improved access to a range of public services and
agricultural credit.
6.03      Devlopint Rural Credit Harkets and Strnxtgh EdSng te  kvvats
Sector Role,. There are about 253,000 small farmer families in Chile, and they
fall into two distinct groups: (a) 148,000 families who farm about 45% of tlhe
agricultural area and produce about 30% of the marketed output (non
minifundistaa): and (b) 105,000 families who farm about 22 of the agricultural
area mostly for home consumption. The latter group supplements ito
suboiltonce Income with wage income from off-farm employment.
6.04      Tle strategy being pursued in this project ic to use a public
sector agency (INDAP) to give Chile's small farmers the opportunity to prepare
themselves for graduation from reliance on public sector credit to reliance on
the commercial banking system for credit, eithor directly or indirectly.
Whether or not farmers uSe the opportunity offered by INDAP to prepare
themselves to meet the requirements of the commercial banks, they will be
dropped as INDAP clients in order to make room for others. During this
transitional phase, they will be participants in the proposed project which
pursues five interrelated avenues to make these farmers more creditworthly from
the perspective of the commercial banking system:
(a)  tItling: where necessary, full title to the farm is given.  This
clearly improves the individual farmer's ability to post
collateral, making him/her a more attractive prospect to a
commercial bank;
(b)  inroving income: particularly through the technology transfer
program a major effort is being made to boost income. Again, this
directly enhances the credit-worthiness of farmers in the eyes of
the commercial banking system;
(c)  Rarticipation in a credit rating system  one of the barriers to
commercial bank lending to small farmers is the cost of collecting
information on such a dispersed group. INDAP will feed credit
information on its clients into a national credit rating system,
called DICOM. Since commercial banks will be able to access this
information easily, it will go a long way toward lowering the cost
of information gathering, and thus make it easier for commercial
banks to meet the credit needs of small farmers with good credit
histories;
(d)  lending at market   tereat rates: for the credit histories of small
farmers to be most relevant as predictors of future behavior, it is
imperative that the smallholders pay market interest rates during
the transition phase to graduation. This is a central feature of
the proposed project; and
(e) study of incentives to encouraae private bank Iending: it is
necessary to understand all the obstacles that must be overcome by
commercial banks before they will expand their coverage to include
the smalJholder sector. For this reason, the Ministry of Financo
will complete a study from this perspective, focussing in
particular on incentives, other than interest rate subsidies or
subloan guarantees, that may be offered to umko smallholdera more
attractive clients to co mercial banks, either as direct clients or
indirectly through agroindustries or other intermediaries.

ANNEX 2.31
Page 1 of 3
CHILE
SMALL FARMER SERVICES PROJECT
INDAP's Strategj, Plan
I.   General Framework
1.        The Government of Chile assumes growth with equity to be a basic
tenet of development, opting for sustainable strategies which make growith and
modernization compatible with the access of all sectors, particularly the
poorest ones, to the benefits of development.
2.        During the past dechde, the accelerated modernization and
development of agriculture has been focussed on the subsector of medium and
large-scale enterprises located in the irrigated Central Valley which,
according to recent studies, generated 90% of the growth experienced by the
sector between 1977 and 1987. During the same period, the technology gap and
conditions of poverty increased in areas containing around 240,000 small
farmers who are basically dependent on agricultural production and who,
together with their families, represent 102 of the national population.
Within this population, indices of rural p'overty reach 55%.
3.        Small-scale agriculture occupies one-third of tots. soil resources,
and represents over one-half of the agricultural production of certain items.
However, many small farmers could reach much greater production levels than
those they have today, if they used more intensively the resources and family
labor they possess, thus substantially increasing their levels of income and
consumption.
4.        INDAP's action is directed towards intensifying small farm
production by makinS possible its integration into the agricultural
modernization process by means of an increased participation in markets,
investment, technology and financing.
5.        The fundamental objective of INDAP's programs for small farmers is
a sustained increase in production and income, giving them the necessary
educational, technological and financial support to assure their participation
in the prevailing opportunities offered by the formal markets for products,
financing and technology. At the same time, INDAP's programs are directed
towards promoting the enlargement of such markets in a manner appropriate to
the specific requirements of small farmers, improving the availability of
services and the demand for goods both needed or offered by them.
6.        INDAP must take three basic goals into consideration to achieve its
objectives:
(a)  Assign top priority to improving the living conditions of the
poorest groups of the population. In this respect, INDAP has
established in its programs a growth strategy which gives preference
to the incorporation of small farmers with the greatest comparative
poverty, but whose main activity and source of income is family-
based agricultural production.
(b) Increase quantitatively INDAP's technical and credit services. It
will be necessary to incorporate a substantial number of farmers



- 45 -
ANNFX 2.1
Page 2 of 3
into these programs in order to effectively modify the exclusivity
characteristic of sector development in past years. In the past,
INDAP's programs did not incorporate more than 14Z of small farmers,
and therefore it is necessary to secure a substantial increase in
order to achieve a level of attention capable of affecting a greater
number of small farmers.
(c) Support processes for the conservation and recovery of natural
resources. There is a high coincidence betweern poverty and the
overexploitation of natural resources. The adequate technification
and capitalization of small farms is, therefore, a necessary
condition for reversing these types of processes.
II. Specific Programs
7.        Technoloev Transfer.  The INDAP technology transfer program
emphasizes educational actions which contribute to small farmers acquiring the
technical and management knowledge necessary to significantly increase the
productivity of their resources and independent capabilities, enabling them to
become part of a self-sustaining process of technological development.
Special consideration is merited for incre.asing the incorporation of peasant
women, with whom the program carries out activities supporting an increased
productive role and facilitating family access to basic services such as
health, education and social subsidies.
8.        The educational attention received by those groups of farmers
joining the program will decrease in time as they go through different phases
until they have achieved the capacity to independently continue the
technological development process through the direct contracting of extension
services in the market. The graduation process will begin immediately for the
larger producers previously attended by INDAP's support program.
9.        A complementary effort will be to improve the technology offered,
extending it in accordance with the specific demands of small farmers. In
this regard, the Agricultural Research Institute (INIA) will begin immediately
to emphasize the generation and transfer of technology oriented to the small
farm sector.
10.       A consequence of this policy is the INIA-INDAP Agreement, which
brings together INIA researchers and INDAP extension workers on a monthly
basis, to work jointly. To carry out this collaborative work, INIA has
established 20 centers of Technological Change and Transfer, which will be
increased to 50 in the coming years in order to cover all agroecological areas
which are important to small-scale agriculture.
11.     : Credit Assistance.  The objective of INDAP's credit program is to
enable small farmers to have access to financial resources, thus facilitating
the incorporation of capital and technology in small farms,
12.       A fundamental criterion in credit policy is to differentiate credit
from subsidies, since in the past INDAP's credit was confused with assistance
granted by the state for different reasons. Thus INDAP is interested in
devising a system of explicit subsidies for the purpose of meeting losses due
to force majeure causes such as droughts, floods and other unforeseen factors
which prevent the sound functioning of credit programs. Small farmers have
great difficulty facing such losses.

13.       A second important critevion is the close coordination of technology
transfor w.lth crudit provlion.  Inh thlis regards market coordinat:-.on
technology level and productive potential are seen as dotermining criteria for
a more professional handling of credit.
1.4.      The incorporation of small producoro into formal marlcets of bank
credit is another important objectivo of INDAP's programs. In thic respect9
through agreenments with commercial Banks, the largest producers capable of
becominig coumercial banlk customers will be graduated. A study will. also be
carried out of other alternatives to promote the expansion of financial
markets for smaller producers, for example, by increasing the number of
financing institutions, the types of financial producto, and the kinds of
services accessible to the small farmer.
15.       Finally, the existe"nce of complementary financing programs carried
out by INDAP through agreements with other institutions should be mentioned.
An example of such a program is the Agreement with FOSIS (Social Solidarity
and Investment Fund) directed towards facilitating the access of small farmers
to subsidies for small irrigation works; and, the Agreement with the Houslng
Ministry which finances the construction of subsidized rural dwellings.
16.       Market Incorporation.  Special effort needs to be directed to
facilitating the access of small farmers to input and output markets.  Access
to markets under reasonable pricing conditions is one of the most serious
constraints on the productive development of small farmers. In this regard9
three types of actions will be carried out:
(a) The coordination of production originating from small farmer groups
with agroindustrial enterprises. Agreements will be made with such
enterprises for the contracting of small farmers' production, backed
by INDAP's technology transfer and credit services.
(b) .The promotion of direct marketing programs for small farmers through
agreements with state institutions which regulate specific markets.
An example is the INDAP-COTRISA wheat marketing agreement.
(c)  The development of associations of producer enterprises oriented
towards solving product storage, processing and marketing problems.
To this effect, credit lines will be developed to finance investment
projects of farm organizations. Similarly, technical services for
pre-investment support will be implemented, as well as the teclnical
support needed to start projects which have been determined to be
commercially feasible.

4.        Based on the preceding table, MIBN's priority objective and social
function in the rural sector centers on regularizing land titles for the
100,000 small farmers with scarce resources, who cannot afford to privately

5.        Acguisition, Management and Disposal of Government Property. In this
area of responsibility, MBN's Regional Secretariats and Provincial Departments
acquire property on behalf of the Government, administer its use through
assignments and concessions, carry out appropriations of national property for
public use, and lease government property. Part of the Ministry's duties also
includes disposing of such property through sales and, in certain cases, free
transfers.
6.        The Ministry also disposes of Government properties, which
constitute a high proportion of the national territory, for purposes of public
interest or for social benefit. However, the Ministry also has the legal
obligation to generate income for the public treasury, and to this end the
Ministry has to sell or rent its properties. The basic concern when disposing
of government property is to proceed efficiently. Much of the Government's
patrimony must remain with the Government to fulfill the functions of public
interest. However, when it is necessary to dispose of such property, the
Ministry:seeks the highest return, administering the sale in the most
efficient manner, and with the objective of securing benefits for the society
as a whole.
7.        Disposing of property as a social action comes tnder the Program to
Grant Free Land Titles for low-income occupants of Government property in
different regions, in accordance with studies prepared by the Ministry's
Regional Secretariats. In this same context, free transfets of Government
property are also made to entities which, after being duly checked, develop
non-profit activities with a high social objective.

Page 3 of 5
8.        Property acquisition and administration has been implemented
according to the Ministry's historical administrative capacity which,
fundamentally, has involved property appropriation. Mechanisms will be
shortly implemented to emphasize a technically more efficient control system,
together with a greater supervisory capacity, to enable collaboration with
other government entities in carrying out the social programs of the
government. The intention is to increase the emphasis on government property
administration in order to support the variety of functions performed by
government institutions. The process of property acquisition must be given
special importance and be streamlined since incorporation of these propertiea
into the government patrimony tends to enhance its social and economic
administration.
9.        National Cadaster of Government Property.  Inventory preservation
and updating of property undeir government administration is a basic objective
of the Ministry. A specific cadastral project was implemented in the Santiago
municipality in coordination with the Municipal Government and the
Architecture Directorate of the Ministry of Public Works, as a pilot venture
which will serve as a model for a complete national cadaster. This cadaster
will be greatly expanded with the incorporation of the regularized private
property contemplated under the mass titling project.
10.       Environment.  MBN ascribes great importance to the environmental
analysis being carried out by the National Environmental Commission, which
will result in the submission of principles for a new National Environmental
Policy. A legislative proposal for the sustainable development and management
of the envirormental patrimony will be prepared and submitted.  The Minister
of National Property presides over this Commission, with the Technical
Secretariat being located in the Ministry.
II.       Settleme-t Policy.  Another function of MBN consists of coordinating
with a variety of other government entities, the preparation of policies aimed
at the utilization and incorporation of government land in the development of
areas of low population density, taking the necessary actions to achieve its
implementation. It also submits and implements policies, plans and programs
aimed at settling such areas.
MBN's Role in Fulfilling its Mandate
12.       In order to consolidate the important objectives outlined in the
preceding paragraphs, necessary changes, both legal and administrative, will
be made in the regulations relating to human and material resources. MBN's
decentralized technical and professional capacity throughout the country,
enables it to carry out its mandate. However, certain adjustments are
required to facilitate more efficient performance and these are already being
undertaken through modernization and rationalization actions.
MBN's Stratezy for the Coming Years
13.       The Ministry of National Property has two priority social
development programs: (a) the regularization of land titles for smallholders;
and (b) the acquisition, adminiistration and disposal of Government property.
MBN's strategy in each case is set out in the following paragraphs.
14.       Land Title Regularization.  The central objective in the
regularization of small holdings, both urban and rural, is to incorporate low-
income persons into the country's developmental mainstream by increasing the

number of properties with legalized land titles, especially in the rural

reconcile the present regulations with an efficient real estate operation that
will give adequate protection to government interests, granting priority to
the direct social benefit of low-income persons and government institutions as
well. The strategy is based on improving the information on properties under
its administrative control and supervision, as well as e%pediting the
procedure of acquisitions by the government, with the purpose of securing an
efficient administration of the goverrment patrimony. The basic objective of
the Ministry's property disposal actions involves a sound knowledge of the
real estate market, and an efficient sales strategy, which aims to bene'it
society as a whole.
18.       The Ministry's social priority with regard to Government lands is to
grant land titles on a fully-subsidized basis to low income persons occupying
those lands, thereby incorporating those lands into the country's productive
development.
A Summary Description of the Ministry's Programs

property,

CHILE
SHALL FARMER SERVICES PROJECT
Technolov Transfer
A. THE SMALL FARM SECTOR!'
1.           An Overview.  The rural population accounts for about 17% of Chile's
12.8 million inhabitants.  Current estimates by the Agricultural Development
Institute (INDAP) divide the fairm population into three ma4or categories:
(a)   an estimated 33,000 farmers who own and operate modern, medium-size and
larger farm enterprises, accounting for about 53% of the agricultural
area and generating 70% of marketed output;
(b)   about 148,000 farm families with 5-12 BIH21, comprising mostly agrarian
reform beneficiaries and small traditional proprietors, who control 45%
of the agricultural area and produce most of the remaining marketed
output; and
(c)   245,000 families, including 105,000 smallholders (minifundistas) with
less than 5 BIH and 1-2% of the arable area and 140,000.other rursl
families, whose main sources of income are sharecropping and wage income
from rural labor.
2.           Small farmers (those in groups (b) and (c) above) play an important role
in the national and regional economies of Chile. They account for some 53% of total
area allocated to vegetables as well as around one-thi, d to cereals, wine grapes and
natural pasture. A limited national agricultural and livestock-survey of 1986/87
demonstrated that some 96% of small farmers are located between the Rio Elqui and
Chilo6 with a marked concentration between fhuble and Chiloe, i.e., between Regions
VIII and X (see Map IBRD 23006). This same study revealed that while the majority
of small farmers are situated in marginal, dry coastal areas and Andean foothills,
over one-third are located in the fertile, irrigated lands of the Central Valley.
These small Central Valley producers generally have better resources and greater
commercial potential than those in the coastal areas and foothills and, therefore,
comprise the bulk of families in category (b) above.
3.           Dependence on family labor distinguishes sharply the smaller from the
larger producer. While all family members work, women and younger family members
are generally unpaid, but adult male member-s receive some form of payment either in-
kind or in-cash. This dependence on family labor explains partially the ability of
small farmers to withstand economic adversity and has also proved invaluable in
sustaining production during seasonal periods when male family members migrate
eitther within Chile or to other parts of the Southern Cone to find seasonal work,
For a moro detailed reviow of the agricutural sector In general and the small tarm subsector in particular, see
Sustainin Growth: An Agricultural Sector Review, IBRID Report.
No. 8759-CH. dated Juno 29, 1990 (Green Cover).
2/    Basic Irrigated Hectare (BIH), a measure of the potential productivity of land relative to that of one hectare of irrigated
land in the fertile Rio Maipo Valley near Santiago. Unirrigated lands are usually 0.14.2 BIH/physical ha.



- 53
ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 14
e.g., fruit-picking, fish-farming and logging within Chile, and sheep-shearing in
Argentina.
4.          Constraints to Growth.  Apart from the small farmers living in the
fertile Central Valley, the soil and climatic conditions in areas of small farmer
concentration are poor. Nevertheless, the higher yields of larger farmers in these
more marginal areas indicate a potential for higher productivity among small
farmers. While medium- and large-scale agricultural entrepreneurs have been able to
invest in new plant varieties, pesticides, fertilizers and modern machinery
generally associated with modern technology, small producers often have lacked such
access owing to: (a) the almost complete absence in Chile of research and
development of technology appropriate to their special conditions (e.g.$ area-
specific, robust crop varieties, crop/livestock/forestry farming systems,
appropriate tools, environmentally sound tillage methods, simple preservation
techniques); (b) lack of resources at the farm-level to pay for available technology
(e.g., certified seed, fertilizers); and (c) resource constraints on public
technical assistance programs. Access to credit is frequently blocked by collateral
requirements which many small farmers cannot fulfill due to, inter alia, lack of -
secure land titles. Further complicating this, smaller producers generally are
averse to borrowing and commercial banks to lending to such clients since they
represent special risks and higher transaction costs.
5.          In addition, small producers lack basic service infrastructure, ir-
cluding collection, storage, processing and marketing facilities. The situation is
further exacerbated by poor secondary roads, especially from Region VIII southwards,
and inadequate transport facilities. For small farmers with marketable surplus,
this often results in ad hoc marketing efforts and vulnerability to middlemen and
price pressures. Such farmers lack community organizations which could act as
conduits to off-farm contacts, offer exposure to productive inputs, product sales
information and outlets, as well as services. Inadequate development and
distribution of basic social services, including sanitation, education and
electrification continue to depress the quality of life for the poorest segments of
the rural population despite special programs and increased social expenditures in
recent years. Further data on the smallholder population are given in Table 1
below.
