B.  Historical Overview of Public Sector  groe__3'
6.       The 1962 Agrarian Reform TJax restructured the public agencies
charged with extension and general assistance to the agricultural sector,  The
Agrarian Reform Corporation (Corporaci6n de Reforma Aeraria_(CORA ) was
established in 1962, and in 1965 wyas assigned the task of administering the
agrarian reform program, including land transfers, technical assistance, and
credit. The Agricultural Development Institute (Instituto de Desarrollo
Agropecuariop _INDAP) was created irI 1962 to provide credit and free technical
assistance to small producers; in 1965 its role was expanded to include such
assistance under CORA-related activities and promotion of small farmer
organizations. In 1967, the Agricultural and Livestock Service (Servicio
Aaricola v Ganadero (SAG ,) was estsblilshed as a semi-autonomous institution
under the Ministry of Agriculture, inter alia, to disseminate technical
information, and to provide technical and administrative assistance to
beneficiaries of the Agrarian Reform Program and small producer-cooperatives.
7.       Consistent with its policy reorientation to reduce public sector
intervention and services, the government in 1973 began a gradual reduction in
resource allocations to the public extension services offered through SAG.
Also, with the termination of the Agrarian Reform Program in 197 , CORA ceased
involvement in agricultural extension in 1974, and eventually was disbanded in
1978. The quality of technical assistance to farmers in the }970s was
generally acknowledged to be substandard.  This, and the government's
deliberate policy oi disengaging from general extension efforts, forced
larger, more commercial producers to acquire technology from domestic and
foreign research and commercial agencies. In 1979, as the SAG extension
program was stopped, farmers were expected to rely fully on these research and
commercial sources for such assistance. To fill the vacuum for small
producers and to encourage the development of private sector services, the
government initiated the Entrepreneurial Technical Assistance Program (ATE) in
1979. Under this program, private consulting firms provided technical
assistance services to farmers, with financing from the M.'nistry of
Agriculture. The quality of services under the ATE program was generally
unsatisfactory, but it did demonstrate that farmers were willing to pay for
technical assistance and that careful selection and supervision of consulting
firms enhanced chances of stuccess. The ATE programu was terminated in 1982.
8.       INDAP continued to provide technical assistance for its small farmer
clientele, albeit with reduced budgetary resources and staffing. Starting in
1979, it made some use of the ATE program to supply such assistance associated
with its own lending operations, and in 1982 introduced a pilot technical
assistance program using subsidized private consulting services in an
agricultural area south of Santiago. In 1983/84 INDAP's program was expanded
to cover 13,140 beneficiaries nationwide. Until 1987/88, the INDAP program of
technology transfer saw only modest growth, principally reflecting resource
limitations and the multi-year nature of beneficiary participation. INDAP's
program was limited initially to small farmers of eight to 12 BIH (Basic
Irrigated Hectares)--a group deemed to have agricultural potential and
viability, to be receptive to improved tec)nology, but to be unable to afford
private technical assistance witlhout governument subsidy.
'Chil!e - Sustelning Grow4th: An Agriculture Sector Review, Volume II', Report 8759.CH, dated June 29, 1990.



55 
ANNEX 3
Page 4 of 14
9.       In 1986, an Inter 15inistcrial Gotu iSiiou for Rxural Development ewas
establishled to formulate and unify public policy to improve the quality of
life for the Chilean rural sector.  The Conmicion recotmended the
implementation of a National Plan for Ruiral Devolopment (Plan), representing a
multi-faceted attack on rural poverty and on the x'ide divergence of social
indicators for rural and urban life.  With lts proposal the Commission sought:
(a) to boost income-generating capacity by modernizing agriculture through
more effective technology transfer, by upgrading the entrepreneurial
capability of producers, by improving the marketing system, and by issuinig
land-titles; and (b) to enhance the quality of life in rural areas by
expanding and making more efficient social programs in the areas of education,
health and housing. Launched in early 1987, the Plan is administered at the
national, regional, and provincial levels, thus ensuring consistency with
policy and budget, but actual implementation, and partial financing, are the
responsibility of local communities.
10.      By law, INDAP beneficiaries must satisfy the following
qualifications: (a) farm less than 12 BIH; (b) have total wealth less than
3,500 UF; (c) obtain income principally from agricultural pursuits, whatever
the land tenancy arrangements; and (d) exhibit distinct potential to improve
agricultural performance. Because of this mandate and its experience in
small-farmer assistance, INDAP has been a Icey participant in executing the
National Plan for Rural Development. Its program of technology transfer has
been expanded and broadened. The program for better endowed small producers
was enlarged by one-third in 1987/88 to service about 18,600 farmers (Program
de Transferencia Tecnol6gica InteRral (PTTI)) (para. 15). Furthermore, in
1987/88 INDAP initiated a special program of technical assistance geared to
the formerly neglected, smaller and poorer segment of the farming population
(Programa de Transferencia Tecnol6gica Bgsica (PTTB)) (para. 16). PTTB
beneficiaries are small, marginal producers considered to have inadequate
resources to achieve self-sufficiency from on-farm activities, in many cases
even after PTTB participation. PTTB, modelled on PTTI, seeks to improve the
quality of life of participating families through medium-term technical
assistance stressing improved use of available on-farm resources and simple
methods to upgrade family health (i.e., diet, hygiene, water treatment, and
improved awareness of social services). In 1987/88, 6,900 families
participated in PTTB, a number which increased slightly to 7,000 in 1988/89.
INDAP considers PTTB as having primarily a social emphasis. In contrast, PTTI
focuses on the productive/commercial development of participants, with social
development arising as a secondary benefit.
The  Mechanics of PTTI and PTTB
II.      The geographical unit on which INDAP's extension programs are based
is a homogeneous agroclimatic zone or area. INDAP prepares an agricultural
development program for each such area ( Pan de Desarrollo A rcola del Area),
based on a detailed assessment of the area's agricultural potential and
constraints, including a determination of the number of potential
beneficiaries for each of INDAP's activities. Private consulting firms are
selected by public tender to deliver extensioni services to INDAP
beneficiaries, from a register of professionally qualified firms maintained by
INDAP. However, non-registered firms meeting INDAP'a qualifications may also
participate in the tenders. A selected firm is required to prepare an annual
work plan, consistent with INDAP norms, proposing a technical program to meet
goals established in the area program (Plan de Trabalo Annual del



- 56 -
ANNEX 3
Page 5 of 14
Extensionista).  This action program is organized around both group activities
(training and demonstration) as well as individual assistance to reinforce
techniques learned in groups and to address farm/family-specific problems.
Farm families are grouped for homogeneity to the extent possible. The number
of individual visits and group activities required of extension workers is
determined annually by INDAP.
12.       uinteagral Program (PTTI).  The PTTI represents a package of extension
activities geared to raising the productive performance and incomes of the
subgroup of small farmers considered to have sufficient resources to not only
achieve self-sufficiency, but to generate a marketable surplus. It endeavors
to upgrade technical skills, to encourage use of new techniques, to improve
the entrepreneurial capability of participants, and to achieve permanent
improvements in both family fiarm income and well-being. Each extension
worker, supported by in-house technical staff, works with three groups of 22
beneficiaries each. The annual extension program consists of eight individual
farm visits, four technical group meetings, three field days, and two
demonstration plots. The annual cost per beneficiary-family is US$250. In
1989/90, 75 private consulting firms participated in PTTI.
13.       Basic Program (PTTB).  The PTTB strives to assist the poorest
segment of the farm population which lacks sufficient resources to reach
self-sufficiency and financial security from on-farm pursuits. The program
attempts to help beneficiaries make better use of available on-farm resources,
to change dietary and hygiene practices in order to enhance family well-being,
and to promote a greater awareness of other available public services.
Technical messages are simple and practical--e.g., use of organic wastes,
better crop rotations, more productive kitchen gardens, more nutritious meal
preparation. Beneficiaries, even after participation, would have little or no
prospect of financial security from farm enterprises, but such participation
sets out to improve their quality of life though better utilization of
resources available. PTTB is much more family-oriented, as opposed to the
producer orientation of PTTI. In PTTB, extension is carried out by teams of
one male worker to assist farmers and one female worker to assist wives.
Teams generally are responsible for four groups of 18 families each.!' The
annual extension program consists of eight on-farm visits, six technical group
meetings, four field days, six kitchen garden demonstrations, and six crop
demonstrations. A recent innovation, in keeping with the now greater emphasis
of PTTB on the family and especially women, has been the introduction of the
Consultora de Apoyo Familiar (CAF) system, made up of female, contracted
professionals (agronomists, veterinarians, etc.) whose role is to train,
evaluate, and coordinate INDAP technicians and the extension firms at the
regional and area levels, in family and women-related issues. The annual cost
per beneficiary-family of PTTB is US$300. INDAP subsidizes the total cost of
PTTB. In 1989/90, 34 extension firms participated in PTTB.
14.       Oualitv Control. INDAP endeavors to maintain high standards in its
extension program. As mentioned above, consulttng firms must meet technical
staffing and professional criteria to gain entry to INDAP's national registry
~/   In areas with better accessibility, each team has more cilents-five groups of 18 families each.

ANNEX 3
Page 6 of 14
for tender participation.5' In addition, training is provided by INDAP both
for its in-house personnel as well as for staff of contracted extension firms.
Most importantly, INDAP strives to ensure quality service to final
beneficiaries. The consulting firm's technical chief (Jefe Tecnico), who
supervises and assists three PTTI extension workers,6' is required to visit
each beneficiary at least once per year. Special consultants (Consultor de
Apoyo T6cnqo (CATI) are stationed in INDAP's area offices to monitor the
quality of work of the private extension firms. Each CAT in paid by INDAP,
but is not an INDAP employee. He or she usually is a native of the area.
Prior to initiating implementation, firms are required to submit to the CAT
for clearance detailed work plans for each extension activity . The CAT or an
INDAP staff-member usually attends each PTTI or PTTB group meeting as an
observer. The CAT reports any major problem to INDAP's Area Chief (Jefe del
Area) for remedial action or for referral to INDAP's regional director, if
warranted. INDAP also requires record-keeping for selected participants, and
monitoring of progress to enable evaluation of results.
15.      Aericultural Research.  INDAP's technical assistance program
benefits from a linkage to agricultural research. The Institute of
Agricultural Research (Instituto de Investipaciones Agropecuarias (INIA)) is
the principal public agency charged with agricultural and livestock research
and its application to the Chilean agricultural sector. While it is a public
corporation, it is expected to function as a private entity (Corporacifn de
derecho privado); it currently derives about 33% of its income from the sale
of products and services. INIA's mandate is to provide research services to
all producers, regardless of size.
16.      In mid-1990, INIA signed an agreement with INDAP to focus its
research on small farmer needs. The aim is to establish a total of about 55
Technology Validation and Transfer Centers (CATTs) throughout Chile to
evaluate packages for small farmers. Formal contacts will be maintained
through the recently created area, regional, and national commissions, which
comprise representatives of INIA, INDAP, consulting firms, and producers. In
addition to INIA, several universities and NGOs are engaged in developing
technology appropriate for small farmers. The Austral University, for
example, has produced a fine variety of potato particularly suited to San Juan
de la Costa in Region X, a difficult agroclimatic area with a large population
of poor farmers.
Results of PTTI and PTTB

efficient model of agricultural extension which minimizes public bureaucracy.
INDAP's staff appear capable and conversant with field-related matters.
Consulting firms participating in the PTTI and PTTB appear well-qualified,
enthusiastic and dedicated. The use of local firms, with staff from the same
areas in which they work, increases understanding of local conditions and

19.         As regards PTTB, considerable investment of time and patience is
required to win the trust of potential PTTB beneficiaries who can ill afford
to risk sparse resources on innovation. For that reason and others, PTTB
emphasizes better utilization of existing resources and seeks to minimize
risks to PTTB families. Its relatively simple technological improvements--
e.g., organic kitchen gardens, raised garden beds, diet diversification,
confined poultry raising, crop rotations--appear to be improving the quality
of life for beneficiaries. In addition, PTTB extension workers are able to
raise awareness of other available social programs--e.g., health services,
housing subsidies, and education programs.
20.         In 1989/90, the number of PTTI participants was cut to about
16,000, or by about 15%, to enable greater resources for PTTB, which was
increased to about 11,000 families in the same year, up from 6,900 in 1987/88,
the year PTTB was started. Farmers dropped from PTTI were offered technical
assistance in the form of technical meetings and field days; however, only
about 875 such producers (25%) opted to participate in these less intensive
activities. During 1990/91, the uumber of PTTI beneficiaries has remained
stable, whMle the number in PTTB has grown to about 17,900. Because of
constrained resources, PTTI and PTTB reach only small fractions of their

target groups. PTTI currently assists about 25% of its potential
beneficiaries, while PTTB currently reaches only 12% of its target
minifundista population. While INDAP has as an objective the graduation of
participants from its programs once the benefits of participation have been
realized, small producers with potential for self-sufficiency and commercial
viability (i.e., PTTI-types) have been important clients of INDAP as well as
essential agents in the development of Chilean agriculture. Therefore, their
graduation from PTTI needs to be carried out based on sotnd criteria. For
different reasons, this applies equally well to PTTB beneficiaries and INDAP's
overall credit program.  To date, such criteria do not exist in practice.  The
1990 Agriculture Sector Review noted that in view of resource limitations, a
complementary measure might be to reassess the appropriateness of current
INDAP assistance criteria (less than 12 BIH and assets of less than 3,500 UF).
Better-off producers could either be phased out, or subjected to considerably
higher cost-recovery. INDAP could also explore ways to make its programs more
cost-effective, and ancillary agencies should improve service delivery.
Recommendations for INDAP and Small Farmer-Related Activities.
21.         Small Farmer Technology.  The 1990 Agricultural Sector Report
identified the following as areas requiring more R&D:
(a)   for crops currently grown (e.g., wheat, beans, potatoes), more
appropriate varieties, which offer more area-specific adaptability
and better performance without heavy use of purchased inputs;
(b)   for small-scale commercialized crop and livestock producers
(i.e., PTTI participants), more and better options for
diversification;
(c)   for production of higher value fruits and vegetables (e.g.,
raspberries, asparagus), a package of practices amenable to
small-scale, labor-intensive production;
(d)   for risk reduction, farming systems research to study options.
including the use of mixed perennials, annuals, livestock, and
forestry activities;
(e)   for easing hardship and increasing productivity, better tools,
including animal-drawn implements;
(f)   for environmental protection, more appropriate tillage methods;
and
(g)   for reducing post-harvest losses and increasing value added,
simple preservation techniques for farm produce.
22.         INIA and INDAP have signed an agreement covering these issues.
Also crop-specific agencies such as Industria Azucarera Nacional Sociedad
An6nima (IANSA), which have considerable technical expericnce wo:king with
small farmers, could assist INIA in the development of farming systems with a
small producer focus. INDAP and IANSA have signed such an agreement, covering
very small producers in Region IX.  Universities which have done selected work
in small farmer technology R&D, as well as NGOs gith relevant experience,
could provide valuable direction for this new priority for INIA. R&D with a



- 60 -
ANNEX 3
Page 9 of 14
small producer focus should be carried out, to the extent possible, on
existing INIA experimental stations in the regions, giving strong emphasis to
on-farm research and demonstrations.  This R&D effort should be accompanied by
a standardized system of feedback to INIA from users of its technology.
Non-Gover_nental Or nizations and Rural Deveglopment
23.         In addition to INDAP's programs of small farmer assistance,
numerous private agencies are engaged in rural development activities in
Chile. These non-governmental organizations (NGOs), numbering about 61 in
1988, arose to fill a vacuum created by the withdrawal of public programs and
personnel in the mid- and late-1970s. Three-quarters of the NGCs currently
operating in Chile were established after 1976. About 70% of NGOs operate in
one or two provinces, and onlv about 30% function in more than three regions.
Many receive financial assistance from external sources.
24.         The principal target group of NGOs are the smallest farmers and
the poorest of the rural population (including indigenous people and
beneficiarieo of the agrarian reform program). As governmental programs were
broadened in the 1980s to include not only small farmers with commercial
potential (PTTI), but also the poorest of the small farmer population (PTTB),
NGOs have diversified in the opposite direction, incorporating small producers
with commercial potential within their scope of activity. NGO projects
generally encompass both productive and social aspects; the relative emphasis
in any particular project depends on the philosophical orientation and the
staffing of the NGO involved. NGOs also engage in a wide range of ancillary
programs, including small farmer technology R&D, and studies on credit and
rural development. Beneficiaries of NGO programs of direct assistance are
estimated at about 15,000 families, approximately 60% of the number of INDAP's
PTTI and PTTB beneficiaries combined, and over double the number of PTTB
beneficiaries alone. The activities of four NGOs working in Chile are
described below. This list is not intended to be comprehensive or
representative, but rather indicative of some of the activities in which these
organizations engage.
25.         "Centre uara el Desarrollo Campesino y Alimentario" (AGRARIA),
which started in 1983, is currently implementing rural development projects in
eight regions of Chile, assisting about 2,800 families with some 200 families
per project. Each project is staffed with four technicians who live in the
project area.  In collaboration with regional agencies and potential
beneficiaries, AGRARIA strives to select project areas and participant-groups
on the bases of representativeness and replicability.  A prospective project
is begun with a detailed study of the project area and its environs, covering
socioeconomic as well as physical/ecological aspects.  Based on this analysis,
an action plan is developed in consultation with small farmer groups in the
area, to.address the technical problems identified. A tailored program of
technical assistance and credit is then implemented. Its projects emphasize a
strict group approach to problem solving, including group meetings of
farmer-participants and demonstrations to learn new techniques. Exchange of
ideas and experiences is encouraged. Credit is provided through a revolving
fund mechanism, and generally for working capital requireme.ats only. It is
supplied in-kind, with the terms and conditions established by each
farmer-group. Loans are settled either in-cash or in-kind, with defaults
handled by group action. AGRARIA's lending in 1986/87 totaled Ch$10.8 million
(US$53,500) with a recovery rate of 93%. Progress is followed throughout
project implementation through detailed M&E. AGRARIA reports that producers



61 -
ANNEX 3
Page 10 of 14
in their projects are obtaining 2.5 tons/ha of wheat, representing an increase
with participation of about 66%. AGRARIA also produces publications on
Chilean agriculture with emphasis on the small farm sector.
26.          "Grupo de  nvestigacionesAR_ag  ias"  CGIA), which was established
in 1978 as a separate group within the University of Christian Hlumanism, has
evolved as an academic unit of development research, communication, and
extension. The principal objective of GIA is the study of the agricultural
sector, with special emphasis on socioeconomic and socio--political aspecto of
small farmer developrnent. Toward this end, GIA conducts regional as well as
nationwide studies on three general themes: (a) agricultur,al modernization;
(b) changes to rural society; and (c) agricultural development
(micro-regional), with emphasis on small farmer production and technology
options. Between 1984 and 1988, CIA designed three development projects in
the provinces of Ruble, Bio-Bio, and Cautin. Among its diverse activities,
GIA began a study in two Mapuche communities of Region IX in 1985, focusing on
the development of a wheat-centered production system (SPAT) capable of
permitting family self-sufficiency. In 1988, GIA undertook to define
methodologies for designing micro-regional development efforts in credit,
technical assistance, training, and organization, using the province of Ruble
as a model (240 families). In a related activity, GIA is carrying out a
three-year study of the agro-ecosystems of' Ruble (Region VIII).
27.          "Instet deromoci6n" (INPROA) was established in 1983 by the
Catholic Church as a private non-profit agency to implement agrarian reform on
Church-owned rural properties. It has since been made independent of the
Church, and now receives grants from Europe and the US for about two-thirds of
its funding. Headquartered in Santiago, it operates seven field offices in
four regions. About two-thirds of its 120-person staff are engaged in field
work. INPROA establishes production and social organizations. Technical and
financial support are then provided to carry out integrated development
activities, including agricultural production, health, housing, and training
of women and youth. It participates in productive ventures with seed capital,
as well as technical and managerial assistance until the people are
self-supporting. Thereafter, it continues to provide accounting, managerial,
and legal assistance. An estimated 4,200 families (including 3,000 Mapuche
families) are benefiting from INPROA-sponsored integrated rural development
projects. About 3,600 families are participating in INPROA's rural housing
scheme, and about 1,900 in its rural health activities.
28.          "El Canela de Nos" is an NCO involved in the development of
improved tools and implements for small farmers. This NGO receives support
from Europeans and holds an annual exhibition where improved tools and
implements are displayed.
29.         Conclusions.  NGOs have played a valuable role in Chile's rural
sector, subst_tuting in part during the 1970s and 1980s for withdrawn public
services to the poor. NGOs continue to be important even as INDAP has
expanded its programs, incltuding those for the poorest within the farm
population. As mentioned above, working with constrained resources, INDAP is
currently reaching but a fraction of its target group. Confronted with this
reality, NGOs and the Government (INDAP) have begun to recognize one another
as partners in the task of assisting the poorer segments of the rural
population. In the past, NGOs did not have a particularly good track record
in coordination, even among themselves, nor in seeking accommodation among

their OwXI dtverso progrvmac/goalo.  This resulted irt inefficicncies and,
ultimately, m.issed opportunities. In addition, NGOs espoused political causes
which thwarted communication with the government. T'his also reportedly
impeded their participation as constulting firms in PTTI and PTTB, and robbed
both key public agencies and NGOs of constructive dialogue aboot their common
goal of rurnl aosistance. Given the present Govarnment's agenda, the proposed

the proposed project, the needad cooperation between NGOs, INDAP and INIA
should be significantly improved.
C. The Proecet
Technoloey Transfer CoMponent
30.          Under the proposed project, technology transfer would no longer be
organized along the lines of PTTI and PTTB. Instead, participants would be
grouped depending on the phase of their participation. All participants would
be either in Phase I (where participants would stay a minimum of three and a
maximum of six years), Phase II (where participants would stay up to three
years), or Phase III (where participants would pay the full cost of the
service provided, and could stay depending on INDAP's capacity to serve them).
In addition, under the proposed project much greater attention and effort
would be devoted to addressing the needs of women. This will include study
and actions to ensure that men and women have equal access to all technology
transfer services provided by INDAP, and that any informal barriers that may
exist limiting wom.n's equal access are identified and overcome.
31.          Organization of Field Extension.  The basic unit would be the
producer group, which would have 18 farmers. In Phase I, each extensionist
would be responsible for a module, which would consist of four producer
groups, for a total of 72 farmers. Modules, in turn, would be grouped in
threes to form multimodules, which would be headed by a Technical Chief,
supported by a female extensionist. In Phase II, the organization of field
extension would be similar to Phase I, except that each module would consist
of five producer groups, for a total of 90 farmers. In Phase I, the training
of extensionists with responsibility for modules would generally be at
technician level, whereas in Phase II the individuals with this responsibility
would be agronomists. INDAP has considerable experience with this method of
organizing its field extension staff and the results to date have been
satisfactory.

Graduation Criteria
36.         At the end of year-3 of the project, an independent evaluation of
the project's technology and credit components would be carried out to
determine appropriate levels of build-up for the second part of the project
implementation period. The goal would be to begin graduating some
beneficiaries from Phase ; to Phase II of the technology transfer program in
year-2 of the proposed project, and for all beneficiaries to be graduated or
dropped from Phase I after six years of participation. Participation in Phase
II would be limited to three years, after which participants would have the
option of participating in Phase III. There would be zero cost recovery in
Phase I, between 20% and 30% in Phase II, and 100% in Phase III.
37.         Criteria for Graduatine from Phase I to Phase II.  The strategy
for graduation would be to graduate individual multimodules, with each having
216 farmers (i.e., 3 modules of 4 groups each, and each group having 18
producers). The focus would not be on graduating individual producers from
within groups. The Area Commission would establish target levels for key
criteria. The indicators/criteria would be:
(a)   Productivit  and Income - Levels higher than the level established
by the Area Commission;



- 65 -
ANNEX 3
Page 14 of 14
(b)   Farm Management Skills - Annual Plans preparad and minimum levels
of accounts maintained;
(c)   Adontion of Technol oq   - Levels higher than the minimum standards
set by the Area Commission;
(d)   Level of Off-Farm Sales and Involvement - Local marketing;
(e)   Level of Involvement in Grou  Activities;
(f)   Extent of Involvement in Women,_Youth and Social ProZrams;
(g)   Prenaredness for Groun Activities in Phase II.
38.         Graduation from Phase II to III.  A decision concerning the
appropriate process and criteria would be made in year-3 of the project.
However, the process and kinds of criteria are expected to be similar to those
used for graduation from Phase I, but the level of advancement would, of
course, be greater, and there would be a sharper focus on marketing skills.
Evaluation
39.           There would be a formal evaluation of the project carried out in
year-3, including an evaluation of the technology transfer component. This
evaluation would be carried out by independent consultants under contract to
INDAP. It would be based on representative samples of producers selected at
that time. Having continuous record keeping on a randomly selected group was
considered and rejected, because the names on the producers in the sample
would inevitably become known to the extensionists and lead to their receiving
particularly good service, which would, in turn, seriously bias the sample.

CHILE
SMALL FARMER SERVICES PROJECT
Rural Women and Youth
Background
1.        The socio-economic impact of rural women in Chile in recent years
has had only sporadic study and is not well-understood. Recent studies have
tended to concentrate on the commercial fruit-growing areas, producing data
manifestly inadequate as a basis for Government policy and program formulation
on behalf of rural women nationwide. Women's participation in the
economically active rural population has increased dramatically in the 1980s,
and there is a strong association between rural poverty alleviation and
women's work in agricultural production and processing. Evidence suggests
that women are earning an increasing proportion of the fami-, income, (up to
45% in some regions), and in a growing number of cases are now the principal
breadwinners--making a critical difference to family living standards and
expectations, and the overall effort to overcome poverty.
2.        In the past, economic planners, legislators and rural development
agencies have tended to reinforce women's domestic role rather than their
economic potential. Many assistance programs have had (and still have) a
"head of household" criterion based on the legal and, importantly, cultural
definiticn of the man as sole provider, which in practice has excluded women.
It is conservatively estimated that some 15% of Door rural households in Chile
are headed by wornen, with striking regional variations ranging from a low of
6% in Region 6 to 28% in Regions 11 and 12. Male migration for seasonal and
longer-term employment is an important factor in this phenomenon. Current
law, however, formally recognizes only widows and the unmarried as legitimate
heads of household, with important implications fcr the access of other
categories of rural women, to government services in their own right. A major
attempt is under-way, spearheaded by the National Women's Service (SERNAM), to
amend law-ys wuhich constrain women, including, importantly, the right to apply
and signi for credit in their orm name.
3.        General Characteristics of Rural Women.  Rural women exhibit great
diversity based on the nature of land tenancy, distinct p-.:oductive systems and
sources of income, regional differences, ethnic origin, and civil status.
Their important role in agricultural production, especially as the principal
food growers, has traditionally been under-reported and under-valued. For
example, women of the communities in the far north ("norte chico") assume full
control of domestic and agricultural activities (livestock husbandry, chiefly
of goats) when the men migrate inter-regionally during years of drought or to
work periodically in the small mines in these areas. Similarly, women of the
Chiloe Archipelago (Region X) take over all agricultural and marketing tasks
during prolonged periods of migration of the men to Argentina, and the far
south of Chile, for livestock management.  Mapuche women have the primary role
in livestock raising and a key role in crop-related act4vities.  In Central
Chile, women are responisible for food production on the "huerta" or kitchen
garden, reise small animuls for sale and consumption, and in substantial areas
of Reglous V and VI grow high value crops such as strawberries on segments of
fmily .1-ai.ds set aside for this purpose. They also perform specific tasks in



67 -
ANNEX 3
APPENDIX
Page 2 of 8
the larger field5, such as sowing/planting, etal.ing, weceding, watering, and
harvesting.  In the Altiplano of Region I, Aymara women perform most of the
agricultural tasks and are essentially supporting the family, for reasons
which are partly economic and partly cultural.!' Lastly, many rural women
increasingly work as salaried employees in fruit, vegetable and grape
harvesting, fruit packaging and agro-industry, for up to six moIlths of the
year.
4.        Total numbers of peasant farm women are estimated at 200,000, and
salaried (temporary or permanent) rural women, about 110,000, with
considerable overlap between the two although precise data is not available.
The former group is generally older (50 years or more), and located
predominantly on minifundios,-which in Chile tend to be concentrated in areas
far from or marginal to, centers of labor demand (fruit-growing and packing
etc), including southern regions and dry coastal areas. Temporary migrant
workers (and off-farm, daily workers) from these areas tend to be male,
leaving a much higher incidence of de facto female heads of household in such
regions. In general, the size and type of small farm correlates directly with
the type and volume of work required of rural women. The smaller the farm,
the more likely is the need for off-farm employment. The family farm is an
integrated production unit, in which labor is divided by sex and age. The
intensity of agricultural work is influenced strongly by the pattern of
cultivation and whether the land is irrigated or dry. Women perform many more
agricultural otctivities in irrigated areas than dry, where agriculture and
livestock husbandry are more extensive.  On the family farm, women's
agricultural tasks run the gamut of those necessarv to operate the farm, with
the exception of zasks involving the use of plows, tractors, pesticide
equipment etc. Women's labor is primarily manual or using small implements.
5.        There are no studies on the number of hours rural women spend on
agricultural tasks, they are not classified as being among the "economically
active" rural population, and their work is not quantified in the census or in
agricultural statistics.- Rural women themselves rarely describe their
agricultural activities as "work", even within the larger farm area, This is
because (a) they do not receive a salary for these activities; and (b) they
are performed essentially in and around the house, in a fluid system of time
allocation and task combinations, which do not generally involve their leaving
the conf'nes of the farm.
6.        In contrast, salaried rural women tend to be younger, more commonly
actual (i.e. legal) heads of household (estimated at 20%), come daily to work
from outlying areas, work less months of the year than temporary males and
have fewer opportunities to supplement this work - some 28% only, have any
regular work in the winter months. Few indigenous womexi work as salaried
labor, partly through tradition, larger family size and frequent involvement
in the home-based production of artisanal items as a source of income. Most
salaried rural women perform little agricultural work on the family farm, and,
like peasant rural women, define themselves essentially as housewives. There

_nivedmentato Strateav Formulation
5.        INDAP has long-held as a goal the participation of women in the
rural economy, but until very recently, none of its programs specifically
encouraged female economic independence. The INDAP program of technology
transfer focussed on women's traditional role as growers of food for family
consumption, as care-giveis and maintainers of basic family living standards.
While the very substantial benefits which have been derived by poor rural
families from this approach a're clear, the reality of rural women's lives on
and off the farm, and their income-earning potential, require a much more
broadly-based strategy and specific attention to gender issues in agricultural
extension and credit. Research suggests that the incorporation of technology
and organizational requirements in development programs has tended to exclude
women's participation.  Further, experience has shown that if rural
development policies and programs do not include specific actions for women,
only marginal benefits accrue to them; conversely, special programs for women
must be carefully linked to the mainstream of general rural development
actions.
6.        Impediments to the development of a strategy for rural women
include the following:
(a)  Technical training in Chile has, traditionally, been directed to
men in tne belief that they are the valid subject for such
training. Howevear, research in diverse parts of Chile has shown
that rural women have a good knowledge of and interest in, the
technical aspects of farm production, and commonly exercise
considerable influence in decisions about planting, inputs and
marketing. Further, female literacy (among young women 30 years
and under), tends to be higher than in males of similar age in
rural areas suggesting potential for more complex technical
education, including, in the field, the INDAP PTTI-type messages
which go well beyond the social sphere.
(b)  A narrow perception and definition of the term "productive"
emphasizes men's agricultural tasks, and discounts or excludes
women's activities, with direct implications for their ability to
obtain INDAP credit, and other than home-based (i.e. social)
technology transfer.A' It is important that extension reach women
in proportion to their participation in the division of labor.
There is a need to detirmirte what that proportion is, and train
extension workers to deliver it.
(c)  The "head of household" criterion (para.2) is a powerful and
pervasive constraint on women's ability to obtain services in their
own right.
yI   The INDAP PTT has three main categories of activities, namely, TechnicaVProductive Development; Development of the
Family and Women; and Peasant Participation. SERNAM asserts that this categorization ImplicHly oeperates women from
t,chnicaVproductive actvities, differentiating between men and women as producers.

(d) INDAP's own charter defines an agricultural producer as inter alia,
one who owns or rents land and has legal title or contract,
qualifications related directly to INDAP's credit activities. Few
women can claim either status, impeding their access to INDAP
credit for income-earning ventures.
(e) There is no current, comprehensive database on rural women. Women
began to be studied by the NGOs in the early 1980s but shortage of
funds inevitably limited such studies to micro-regions. Much of
the data is considered out of date and not an appropriate basis for
national policies for the 1990s. Recent studies have focussed on
fruit-growing areas but most peasant farm women are not working in
fruit and similar problems of apprbpriateness apply.
(f) While the situation is clearly improving, especially at the central
level, there remains a general lack of awareness among INDAP
managers and extension contractors, about gender issues in rural
development.  This is accompanied and exacerbated by the emphasis
in extension training curricula, on technology rather than
methodology.
(g) A comprehensive, proven methodology is needed for opening up
income-earning opportunities for peasant women which takes into
account their diversity, multiple roles and acute constraints of
time and place.!' The elements of such a methodology undoubtedly
exist already in the experiences of the NGOs, but INDAP lacks the
internal and field staff resources and expertise necessary to
collate, rationalize and test it in the short-term.
(h) The institutional orientation towards rural women is still very
new. Professionals in INDAP working full-time on establishing the
gender issue in the rural development mainstream, are few, and
their current position in the bureaucratic heirarchy is marginal
and somewhat tenuous. SERNAM can be helpful in keeping the gender
issue on the front burner, but the so-called "women's unit" in
INDAP needs to develop greater weight and political support within
the institution.
7.         There are encouraging signs of change, however.  INDAP now has a
formal agreement w1th SERNAM, and contractual relationships with major
NGOs,!' and is making an important effort to address many of the above issues
and incorporate the gender perspective into its technology transfer and credit
programs. Progress has been slow, chiefly due to a combination of patchy
commitment, especially at the Regional level, and shortage of resources. New
programs are still in their infancy and merit full support, to achieve
momentum, critical mass and sustainability. The following paras set out the

objectives.

have already started on a modest scale and more are planned. GIA,
a major NGO, recently completed under contract to INDAP, an
intensive _ourse for 75 extensionists. The cost appears to have
been prohibitive and future courses will probably be less intensive
and have greater coverage. INDAP has an important role in pressing
for change in the way extensionists are trained--that is, shifting
the emphasis from technology to extension communication
methodology, which is expected to have important implications for
the way women's on-farm activities are viewed.Y

9.        Credit.  INDAP acknowledges that men have traditionally been given
priority under its credit program (tied closely to the already-mentioned head
of household, productive activities, and agricultural producer criteria).
Further, it has been, and still is, difficult for a woman to obtain INDAP
credit if her partner already~ has a loan, no matter how sound the proposal.
(In a system where credit demand exceeds availability, rationing credit on the
basis of gender and perceived relative importance will need to be monitored).
There is no data on how many women have received INDAP credit in the past,
although there is broad agreement that women tend to have a superior repayment
record. Efforts have been made to ensure that CREDAP tracking of INDAP credit
disaggregates data by gender.
10.       Important changes have already been made in INDAP's credit
regulations, to acknowledge the reality of women who are head of household and
managing the entire enterprise, through the absence of their partner. This
situation affects a high proportion of poor rural women in Regions 11 and 12,
where they can now apply for INDAP credit in their own name, without the
signature of their partner. Collateral requirements have also been changed
(affecting men as well) to permit guarantees in the form of animals and
machinery, co-signature of relatives and neighbours etc. No collateral is
required for loans of up to 15 UF (working capital) and 30 UF (investment
credit) respectively. It appearss however, that the application of these new
rules varies greatly between Regions -- Avmara women in Region 1 have been
repeatedly denied credit by INDAP despite their status qualifying them for the
same tre.tment as the women described above. A special effort is needed to
ensure that the Regions understand the changes and that they are applied
consistently.
I1.       Women's Micro-Enterprise Development.  A special problem exists
with women applying for credit for on-farm micro-enterprises, even on a group
basis (now favored by INDAP) due to restrictions on eligible activitl'-.
Women frequently need credit for activities which are prcdu;Live/income
generating but not specifically agricultural, including artesanal items,
blankets, fruit drying and packaging, and baking. Rather, value is added to
raw and semi-processed agricultural materials, and the credit is needed to
acquire basic equipment (ovens for baking, looms for blankets) and/or to
purchase-materials. Many rural women are confined to the farm, especially to
care for children, and have no option but to try and generate income in situ.
Other women pick and pack fruit for several months a year but are virtually
immobilized for the remainder of the year due to the lack of opportunities in
their areas. IN@DAP is looking at broadening the list of eligible activities
but, again, ambivalence is evident. Pilot micro-enterprises (mostly in
groups) are under consideration, using consultants to detirmine project
feasibility in preparation for credit applications, and subsequently carry out
training of successful credit applicants in simple bookkeeping, marketing and
skills related to the nature of the project.

Rural Youth
12.       Backerotud.  The need to incorporate rural youth into INDAP's plan
of action for poor rural families is increasingly vital and needs to be
reflected in special policies. Rural youth has a demonstrably high potential
to leave the land and migrate to the cities. The dilemma for planr.ers is
whether- to erncourage young men and women to remain on the land or provide
incentives for a future elsewhere (training, job creation strategies etc.)
Research reveals that elements such as the lack of modernity in rural life,
and the observed hardships of their parents, are sufficient incentive to
propel young people to the cities where they find their rural skills largely
unusable, and their prospects poor. A small, but increasing number is going
on to tertiary education incl'uding university. There has been in the 1980's
an observed tendency among rural families to free children from farm-related
daily obligations in favor of staying in school, studying at home etc. as a
means of ultimately escaping rural life. In any case. man7 small farms cannot
support more than one family and youth is compelled to leave.
13.       The average age of heads of household participating in INDAP PTT is
53.4 years. While this clearly signals the need for special TT and credit
programs for youth, programs already tried by NGOs indicate that such training
can have the reverse effect. INFROA trained 400 youth leaders over 4 years to
work with their peers in developing modern approaches to rural life. Of those
trained, some 350 migrated to urban centers. The solutions to these issues
are thus not simple. Poor rural youth are estimated to number some 600,000
persons, the majority of which are considerably better-educated than their
parents.
14.       INDAP's Response - PTT Fomento.  In response to e growing
perception that better-educated rural youth are not only promising subjects
for more complex technical training, but can be a central element in the rural
development strategy, INDAP is already implementing a pilot program for the
18-30 year age cohort calle3 PTT Fomento (PTTF), focusscd initially on the
Metropolitan Region. The new title distinguishes it from the regular PTT
since most rural youth, like women, do not qualify technically or legally as
"agricultural producers" under the land title or rental contract criteria.
15.       INDAP has contracted local agronomists to provide a high-intensity
TT of 30 visits per year to work with groups averaging 5 members. The groups
cultivate carefully-selected strains of tomatoes under "hothouse" conditions
(i.e., under plastic) for sale in regional centers in the off season when
tomatoes are normally unavailable.   The technology requires only small
amounts of land--each member of the group cultivates one "nave" (6x30 meters)
and each hothouse can be made up of several naves--which the participants rent
from their parents!' for a token amount per annum, since most youth do not
own land. The program actively encourages the participation of persons whose
parents do not have land, but who are willing to learn the technology and
provide labor. The training provided these grouDs also includes market:ng,
accounting and decisionmaking. The technology permits a second, comple.icnetary
crop each year. usually of corn. One full-season crop of tomatoes frow one
nave has been estimated at around 2,000 kg, which would net CH$450.000 at
QI   Partiolpants' parents do not have to be INDAP PiT beneficlaries.

regions, focussing on milk processing ventures.
16.       The current crop of PTTF groups contains no female participants.
The reasons for this are still unclear, although families' traditional view of
appropriate activities for young, unmarried rural women may inhibit their
involvement. INDAP intends to focus on young married couples in future
expansion of PTTF. In general, the emphasis for young rural women must be on
technical training, removing long-standing biases which preclude their
eligibility, and viewing them as agents of rural change by educating them to
use modern tools, machinery and techniques.

CHILE
SMALLA FARMER SERVICES PROJECT
Land Titling Services
I;ackgound
1.          Land titling of small farmers has been carried out by the Ministry
of National Property (Ministerio de Bienes Nacionales (M3N), for some thirty
years under the authority of varicus laws and until the early 1980s, at modest
levels.  An assessment in 1973-1975 determined that approximately 200,000
rural families and 80-100O,00Ourban families were occupying land for which
they lacked title.  In 1978/79 legal changes were enacted and resources
appropriated to complete land regularization nationwide by 1985. These legal
changes included transferring responsibility for titling holdings in
indigenous areas of Regions VIII, IX and X to INDAP. While the process of
titling was not completed by the date anticipated, around 108,000 rural titles
and 64,000 urban titles were regularized by MBN (Table attached), and about
72,100 by INDAP on indigenous lands, by the end of 1990. It is estimated that
around 110,000 non-indigenous and about 6,300 indigenous rural families still
await title out of the originally-identified number.1' Recent analysis,
however, has updated these estimates to include some 20,000 additional titles
needed for farmers occupying fiscal (i.e., government-owned) lands.
2.          Possession of a secure land title carries important benefits for
rural families including: (a) an immediate and substantial increase in
property value; (b) access to INDAP credit (i.e., collateral) and agricultural
extension; 1 (c) eligibility for subsida.zed housing construction under
Ministry of Housiag programs; (d) access to forestry subsidies under D.L. 701;
and (e) eligibility for irrigation water rights and subsidies for small
irrigation worles. In general terms, possession of title brings small farmers
a genuine and rapid degree of economic stability and security and can
significantly enhance a family's access to development opportunities.
Further, properly titled land provides a demonstrated incentive for increasing
small farmer use of credit and extension services.
The   N  TitAlin  ProErm
3.          The underlying legislation and internal M3N administrative
procedures were examined extensively, including by a consultant with expertise
in Latin American land titling programs. Procedures reviewed included the
following: (a) the preliminary identification of eligible properties and

4.          Legal Basis.  IBN's titling activities are governed by several
laws the principal of which being: (a) D.L. No. 2.695 of May 1979 which sets
out the basic norms of eligibility to apply for title;!' and (b) D.S. No.210
of March 1983 which establishes the organizational requirements and standards
governing the register of priVate contracting firms which handle the surveying
and legal activities of titling for MBN. A third law, D.F.L. No. 5 of 1967
and its subsequent amendments pertain to titling activities among traditional
agricultural communities (non-indigenous), chiefly in Region IV and to a
lesser extent in Regions I and V, which are regarded as having special
characteristics requiring separate procedures. Virtually no titling has
occurred yet in these communities owing to philosophical and economic debate
about whether and how communal lands should be divided. The first two laws
are being studied to overcome impediments 'to their interpretation and
application, while the law relating to traditional communities is likely to be
modified, although in what form remains unclear.
5.          Eligibility Recuirements.  The following basic standards govern
eligibility for application for a title urder the MBN program: (a) continuous
and exclusive occupation of the plot for at least five years; (b) the plot
should have a fiscal. value not exceeding 800 territorial tax units (around
US$16,800)3'/ (c) no pending claim or lawsuit disputing the applicant's
rights to possess the plot; (d) definable borders in the form. of fences,
natural barriers, etc; and (e) the applicant should be able to produce
specific forms of documentation substantiating the claim of possession.
6.          Register of Private Contractors.  Only firms on MBN's Register of
Private Contractors are eligible to tender for its titling contracts.
Requirements for inclusion in this register are as follows: (a) the firm must
have already performed legal or topographical work under the ti.tling laws, or
similar work; (b) legal and technical staff must have the necessar y
professional qualifications including a university degree and at least two
years of practical professional experience; and (c) the firm must possess the
technical instruments and vehicles necessary to perform the work and be in
verifiabl.e sound financial condition.!' The Register is reviewed annually
and firms a^:e dropped if performance is deemed inadequate. MBN cut the
Register back from 66 to 33 firms in early 1991 on the grounds of inefficiency
and insufficient financial and technical capability, but has since built the

7.          The system of using private contractors operates at two levels:
(a) through fully subsidized and contracted mass titling programs using
resources of the National Regional Development Eund (Fontdo Nacional de
Desarrollo Regional (PNDR), the Solidarity and Soel Investment Fund (Fondo
do Solidaridad e Inversi6n Social (FOSIS) and 5% of the revenue raised from
kBN'a annual sales of government property;6' and (b) through direct contract
between the applicant anid the private firm, with costs borne by the applicant
except for the costs of MBN supervision, which is required by law in both
(a) and (b). The bipartite scheme ensures that MBN subsidies go to the most
needy while not excluding better-off families from the benefits of an
organized, supervised process.
8.          The Process.  In general, individuals in need of title are
identified through the collaborative actions of the Regional offices of MBN
and the municipalities. It appears, however, that in a substantial number of
cases, the individual initiates contact with local authorities after
discovering that proper title is a prerequisite to qualify for a
government-subsidized service, most commonly, housing. A simple evaluation of
the prospective applicant's ability to pay (usually vouchsafed by a social
worker familiar with the family) determines whether MBN will subsidize either
in full or in part the survey and legal work.2' The private firms submit
proposals to MBN including cost and a timetable of activities. Fees for some
activities, especially legal, are set by law. The awarding of a contract sets
a chain of actions in motion which can take anywhere from nine months (an
ideal case without complications) to two years to complete. Around 2Z of
title applications fail en route because problems overwhelm the process.
9.          The major steps to obtaining title are:
(a)   The applicant's documentation is checked for legal validity, to
which he/she makes a sworn declaration. A draft legal report is
issued authorizing (or not) further processing of the application;
(b)   The plot is surveyed and mapped, and a detailed written
description is prepared. The topographical work is reviewed,
including in situ inspections by HEN technical supervisory
staff;!'/
(c)   These two documents (i.e., draft legal report and physical
description) are submitted with a technical report for legal
review by the firm's legal staff, who prepare a final report which
is then scrutinized by MBN legal supervisors. If satisfactory, a
By law, use of the 5% ftom sales of government property Is restrilcted to MBN's own thling activitXes, or to the costs of
supervising the contractors. It cannot be used to pay contractors' fees.

(d)   Third parties opposed to the proposed action due to competing
claims must submit their protests in writing to the regional
office of MBN within 30 days of its second publication. Such
claims are heard in a civil court within the relevant
jurisdiction. In a very small number of complex cases, the
involvement of tne Ministry in Santiago is required;
(e)   If no challenge is received, the property is registered in the
Conservador de Bienes Raices (property roll). Such property
cannot be divided or sold for one year except to pay creditors or
for public purposes. After one year of uninterrupted possession,
full property rights ure granted to the applicant; and
(f)   In some cases opposition surfaces after property registration.  If
the appropriate Regional Tribunal vindicates the claim, the new
title may be overturned or financial compensation paid by the
title-holder to the successful claimant.
10.         A Critique of the HEN Proaram.  The review concluded that the
program has worked very well, and that legislation analogous to D.L. Z.695
could provide a model for similar undertakings in other parts of the Region.
The program is simple, cost-effective and politically non-contentious, MEN
titles are legally sound, and small farmers desire and have confidence in
them. MBN regulations and administrative procedures are both transparent and
reasonably efficient, while its titling staff levels, at the centre and in the
regions, are admirably lean. Ownership interests are identified with care and
disputes are resolved responsibly.9' Property boundaries are measured and
marked, titles are issued and recorded properly, and convey full ownership to
land occupants.
11.         However, shortage of resources in recent years has reduced the
numbers of subsidized cases being processed annually to low levels, with the
prospect of making the current backlog permanent. Regional allocation of FNDR
funds (para. 6) to titling has declined sharply as health, education and
housing have assumed top priority. MBN regional offices have been attLmpting
to carry out titling activities using their own personnel arLd resources as a
means of keeping the program moving, in the absence of adequate funds to pay
private firms. Progress has been slow. In Region V, where processing of some
3,000 out of 6,000 titles underway had to be stopped for lack of funds, the
Regional Secretariat of MBN initiated a pilot program with the Municipality of
Valparaiso to pool available resources and complete titling of the poorest
applicants among the group stalled.

equipment, causing delays in the processing of applications, especially in

13.         Title Cost.  The current cost of processing a title averages from
UJS$100-200.-7 ~The cost is lower for individuals located in ccmmunities
where the terrain is easily handled and applicants live in close prox'rmity
permitting mass or group titling. For more isolated families in difficult
terrain or where a third party challenge requirt.s follow-up legal and survey
Work, the cost rises.  MBN's cost estimates compare fairly closely with those
of INDAP (para. 23). Cost estimates for titling under t.;e proposed project
show an average of US$171 per title. An objective of the project is to ensure
that a high proportion of the more difficult, and thus higher cost, cases are
completed.
14.         Title Insura_ce or Gua-antees.  The cost of MEN titling programs
w7as reviewed in the context of the potential benefits of providing title
insurance or government guarantees to MBN clients.  The anticipated project
rationale for proposing such guarantees was: (a) that the cost of MBN
supervision--essentially the pre-screening of clients, the verification of
property boundaries, and the detailed review of documentation--would be founud
to exceed the probable cost/risk of claims for erroneous title approvals; anid
(b) that the time required for close MBN supervision would lead to significant
delays in project implementation.
15.         It was concluded that the MEBN program is already managed in a
cost-effective manner, and that MEN staff levels are already so lean that no
meaningful unit cost reductions could be achieved through title insurance
economies.  While the MBN does supervise, on averaget over 70Z of title

for MBN services, direct state guarantees appear particularly appropriate.  In

17.          MBN is currently reviewing a number of elements of the program to
ensure greater efficiency, as follows: (a) the titling laws and applicants'
responsibilities are not well understood outside official and professional
circles and MBN is looking at ways of clarifying these, e.g., through the tise
of short, topical statements on local radio; (b) I-MN is fully-aware of the

process to aveid duplicaticn of effort, especially between the private firms
and MBN supervisors.  1,W

19.         The Private Contractors.  D.L. 2.695 and D.S. 210 place
operational responsibility for direct titling activities with the private
sector. Firms registered with the MBN seek D.L. 2.695 titling cases as a
regular part of their wider business, irrespective of the availability of
MBN-subsidized work (para. 6). They depend directly on the relatively modest
income-eaniing potential of this work (profit margins rarely exceed 25%) for
only part of their income, but it is an important catalyst to the generation
of new business in their areas and thus acts as an incentive to quality
perfor=ance. In an important sense, the role of these firms is crucial to the
"institutionalization" of project accomplishments. They, even more than the
Ministry, have the incentives', local knowledge, and financial structures to
achieve short and longer-term titling goals.
20.         Local knowledge appears to be a critical consideration to an
effective titling program. While concentrations of potential clients can
indeed be identified through MBN field surveys, (easily in the case of
densely-populated areas), surveys are not good instruments for prioritizing
titling action. They tend to identify the easiest cases, the outcome of which
can be the progressive marginalization of potential clients who represent
social or geographical problems. In contrast, local firms, in addition to
handling the easier cases, are well-positioned to identify and shepherd
problem cases through because they are involved regularly with the business of
the ccm=unity.  Under the proposed project, the opportunity to process    I
comparatively larger numbers of clients, already identified by the MBN, along
with assurances of direct payment by the Ministry, is likely to attract the
interest of large firms with little or no knowledge of local conditions. The
Ministry and tne Bank should be aware that MBN contracts might endanger the
permanent, longer-term titling capacities that can only be provided
economically by smaller, local firms. This may devolve to a procurement
issue. Given the substantive importance of developing a sustained titling
capacity in rural areas, a requirement that firms bidding on MBN contracts
demonstrate a solid knowledge of local conditions, would appear to be a
legitimate requirement.
21.         Women and Titling.  Women can own land in their own name in Chile,
if they derived it through inheritance or purchased it independently as legal
head of household. Land acquired by a married couple is registered in both
names.  A concerted effort is underway, through a formal agreement between MEN
and the National Women's Service (SERNAM), to focus land titling efforts,
inter alia, on female heads of household, who are believed conservatively to
number some 15% of rural families nationwide, and generally fall into the
lowest echelon of income in rural Chile with the greatest need for access to
the kinds of subsidized government services for which title is either required
or advantageous.  There is no disaggregated data on how many title recipients
in past years were women, but the project has set annual targets for titling
of women heads of household based on data provided by SERNAM (see Annex 7).
The proposed retroactive study will try to reveal the former, while monitoring
and evaluation follow implementation of the latter.
INDAP's Titlinl2 for the Indigenous
22.         Legal Basis.  INDAP is responsible for titling indigenous lands
under D.L. No. 2.568 of March 22, 1979. This modified the pre-existing Law



81 -
ANNEX 4
Page 8 of 9
No. 17.729 of September 1972 which had established the Institute for
Indigenous Development, an agency with comprehensive responsibilities
including the disposition of indigenous lands. Its titling functions were
handed over to INDAP under the new law of 1979 and its remaining functions
dispersed among various other agencies. A critical aspect of the 1979 law was
the requirement that indigenous lands be divided and titles be granted
individually, in contrast to the indigenous tradition of communal holdings and
farming arrangements.
23.         INDAP's titling activities are concentrated in the Mapuche
communities in Regions VIII, IX and X. As of September 1990, 77,110 titles
had been given, with an estimated 6,250 still to be done. In contrast to the
system of private contractors operated by MBN, INDAP regional personnel
handles all legal and survey work itself. While the average cost of a title
compares closely with MBN at just over US$100, the process takes much longer,
averaging from 2.5 to 3.0 years, three-quarters of which is the legal portion.
(INDAP admits that these figures for time taken indicate an ideal case, which
is uncommon, and the process generally takes even longer). This reflects the
complex task of establishing a fiscal value for a communal holding, dividing
it rationally among families and compensating family members who are not given
title but who would, under indigenous custom, have equal rights to its use.
Only seven INDAP legal staff are assigned 'to the indigenous titling program.
The process is hampered by frequent challenges, errors, lack of vehicles and
paucity of resources to compensate community members who do not get title--
such compensation required by law.
24.         Pendine Chang s.  The Government set up the Special Commission for
Indigenous Peoples (Comisi6n Especial de Pueblos Indfgenas (CEPI) in April
1990 to review all laws pertaining to the indigenous, seeking to move away
from the concept of assimilation into the general society, towards greater
recognition of their cultural diversity and potential. While INDAP is
continuing to process indigenous titles at low levels, responsibility for this
work may ultimately be transferred to another agency, possibly MBN. A new law
on the indigenous is in draft and under discussion. It is anticipated that
the matter of indigenous lands will have priority and cover all indigenous
groups in Chile, not just the Mapuche. The fairly numerous Aymara people in
the Altiplano region of the Northeast border, for example, present thorny
problems of land tenure and incorporation into the Chilean political and
economic system. The draft legislation provides for the establishment of a
National Corporation for Indigenous Development (Corporaci6n Nacional de
Desarrollo Indigena (CONADI), repc-ting directly to the President. Because
indigenous-related policy is currently under review and changes to public
programs are likely, no indigenous titling has been included in the project.
